Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
Response to the Second Objection: The Definer and the Parts of Essence
Sanusi responds to the objection that the definer must inform of the parts of the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a definition does not need to introduce new knowledge about the parts of an essence, and how the definer functions as a means to present the parts in a structured whole.

Core Idea
A definition arranges known parts, not introduces unknown ones.

Sanusi addresses an objection that the definer must inform about the parts of the essence. He argues that the parts may already be known; the definer's role is to present them in an ordered, distinguished set. The distinction between definition and defined is one of detail versus totality. This lesson unpacks that response and the supporting analogy of social form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi begins by clarifying the relationship between definition and defined as detail vs. totality.
The initial part sets up the response.
2
Understand the core claim
Sanusi denies that the definer must inform of the parts, because the parts may already be known. The definer brings them together in an ordered, distinguished set.
3
Grasp the distinction between knowledge of essence and knowledge of parts
Knowledge of the essence is not the same as knowledge of its parts as separate conceptualizations. The parts must be observed collectively.
4
Consider the social form analogy
The social form (الهيئة الاجتماعية) is the cause of the compound obtaining but not of any individual part. This illustrates how the definer relates to the defined.
This analogy is key to understanding Sanusi's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجود الواحد، تارة جملة، وتارة شيئاً فشيئاً، ولم يرد في حل الإشكال على أن الحد مجموع الأمور التي كل واحد منها مقدم، ولا يجدي نفعاً لأن المحدود أيضاً كذلك، فلا بد في بيان المغايرة والسببية من أن يقال تلك الأمور من حيث الملاحظة تفصيلاً حداً وإجمالاً محدوداً وهو معنى كلامهم . وأما عن الثاني : فبأنا لا نسلم أن معرف الماهية يجب أن يعرف شيئاً من أجزائها، لجواز أن تكون الأجزاء معلومة، ونفتقر إلى حضورها مجموعة مترتبة ممتازة عما عداها ويكون ذلك بالمعرف وحاصله أن الماهية وإن كانت نفس الأجزاء بحسب الذات لكن لا يلزم أن يكون العلم بها هو العلم بالأجزاء، بمعنى التصورات المتعلقة بها، بل لا بد من ملاحظتها مجتمعة متميزة عن الأغيار، ويجوز أن تبقى الأجزاء مجهولة ويفيد المعرف بالذات، ويعود التغاير إلى الإجمال والتفصيل، كما في تعريف الماهية بأجزائها أو غيره، ويصح التعريف بالخارج على ما سيجيء وبما ذكرنا يندفع ما يقال إن أجزاء جميع الماهية نفسها، فكيف لا يكون العلم بها علماً بها؟ وأن معرف الشيء سبب المعرفته، أي حصوله في الذهن، فكيف لا يحصل شيئاً من أجزائه ..؟ وأن علة حصول الشيء لو لم تكن علة لشيء من أجزائه لجاز حصول كل جزء بدونه، فجاز حصول الكل بدونه فلم يكن علة ولنعتبر بالهيئة الاجتماعية فإنها علة الحصول المركب وليست علة لحصول شيء من أجزائه . وأما عن الثالث : فبأنا لا نسلم أن التعريف بالخارج يتوقف عن العلم باختصاصه بل على الاختصاص نفسه، فإن الذهن ينتقل من تصور الملزوم إلى تصور لازمه الذهني، وإن لم يتقدم العلم باللزوم، ولو سلم فيكفي في ذلك تصور الشيء بوجه، وتصور ما عداه إجمالاً، كما في اختصاص الجسم بهذا الحيز، وإن كان مبنياً على امتناع كونه في حيزين، واشتغال حيز بمتحيزين، وإلى هذا التسليم نظر من قال : الوصف الصالح لتعريف الشيء يجب أن يكون لازماً بين الثبوت لافراده، الانتفاء وبين عن جميع ما عداه، وينبغي أن يعلم أنه وإن كان لازماً بحسب الصدق لكن لا بد أن يكون ملزوماً بحسب التصور، وأجاب بعض المحققين عن الأول بمنع كون جميع أجزاء الماهية نفسها، بل جزم بأنه باطل تمسكاً بأن الأشياء التي كل واحد منها متقدم على الشيء يمتنع أن يكون نفس المتأخر . ثم قال : ويجوز أن يصير عند الاجتماع ماهيته هي المتأخرة، فتحصل معرفتها بها، كما أن العلم بالجنس والفصل وبالتركيب التقييدي متقدم على العلم بالجنس المقيد بالفصل وهي أجزاؤه، وبها يحصل العلم به، ورد المنع تارة بدعوى الضرورة، وتارة بالاستدلال بأن جميع أجزاء الشيء إن لم تكن نفسه فإما تكون خارجة عنه وهو ظاهر البطلان أو داخلة فيه فتركب الشيء منها ومن غيرها، فلا تكون هي جميع الأجزاء بل بعضها، وأيضاً لو كان الشيء
The single existent, sometimes as a whole, sometimes gradually, and did not intend in solving the problem that the definition (حد) is the sum of things each of which is a premise, and it does not avail because the defined (المحدود) is also like that, so in explaining the distinction and causality it must be said that those things from the perspective of detail are the definition and from the perspective of totality are the defined, and that is the meaning of their statement. As for the second [objection]: we do not concede that the definer (معرف) of the essence must inform something of its parts, because it is possible that the parts are known, and we need to have them present in an ordered set distinguished from others, and that is achieved by the definer; the gist is that the essence, even if it is the same parts in terms of essence, it does not necessarily follow that knowledge of it is knowledge of the parts, meaning the conceptualizations related to them, but rather they must be observed collectively distinguished from others, and it is possible that the parts remain unknown and the definer informs by essence, and the difference reverts to entirety and detail, as in defining the essence by its parts or other than them, and definition by extrinsic property (التعريف بالخارج) is valid as will come, and by what we have mentioned is repelled what is said: that all the parts of the essence are itself, so how is knowledge of them not knowledge of it? And that the definer of a thing is the cause of its being known, i.e. its mental existence, so how does it not obtain any of its parts? And that if the cause of obtaining a thing were not a cause for any of its parts, it would be permissible for each part to obtain without it, so it would be permissible for the whole to obtain without it, so it would not be a cause; and consider the social form (الهيئة الاجتماعية) for it is the cause of the compound obtaining and not the cause of any of its parts obtaining. As for the third [objection]: we do not concede that definition by extrinsic property depends on knowledge of its specificity, but on the specificity itself, for the mind transfers from the conceptualization of the necessitator to the conceptualization of its mental consequent, even if knowledge of the implication is not prior, and if conceded, then sufficient in that is the conceptualization of the thing in one respect and the conceptualization of what is other than it in general, as in the specificity of a body to this space, even if it is based on the impossibility of its being in two spaces and a space occupying two bodies, and to this concession referred the one who said: the attribute suitable for defining a thing must be a necessary concomitant (لازم) between affirmation of its individuals and negation from all others, and it should be known that even if it is necessary in terms of truth, it must be a necessitator (ملزوم) in terms of conceptualization. And some investigators answered the first [objection] by denying that all parts of the essence are itself, rather asserting it is false, adhering [to the argument] that things each of which is prior to a thing cannot be the later thing itself. Then he said: and it is possible that upon aggregation its essence becomes the later, so its knowledge is obtained by them, just as knowledge of genus and differential and by restrictive composition is prior to knowledge of the genus restricted by the differential, and they are its parts, and by them knowledge of it is obtained; and they rejected the denial sometimes by appealing to necessity and sometimes by inference that if all parts of a thing are not itself, then they are either external to it which is obviously false, or internal to it so the thing is composed of them and something else, so they are not all the parts but some of them; and also if the thing
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a continuous response to objections regarding the nature of definition (حد) and the definer (معرف). It addresses two specific objections (الثاني and الثالث) and includes an additional response from investigators. The author begins by clarifying a point about the definition being the sum of things each of which is a premise, explaining that the distinction between definition and defined is one of detail versus totality. Then, responding to the second objection, he argues that the definer of an essence does not necessarily have to inform of its parts, because the parts may already be known; instead, the definer brings them together in an ordered set distinguished from others. He explains that knowledge of the essence is not identical to knowledge of the parts as separate conceptualizations; rather, the parts must be observed collectively. He refutes claims that all parts of the essence are itself, using the analogy of social form (الهيئة الاجتماعية), which is the cause of the compound obtaining but not of any individual part. Addressing the third objection about definition by extrinsic property (التعريف بالخارج), he asserts that such definition does not require prior knowledge of the property's specificity; the mind transfers from the necessitator (ملزوم) to its mental consequent (لازم) spontaneously. He then discusses the conditions for a suitable defining attribute: it must be a necessary concomitant (لازم) in truth but also a necessitator (ملزوم) in conceptualization. Finally, some investigators answer the first objection (likely mentioned earlier) by denying that all parts of the essence are itself, using an argument about priority and posteriority. They illustrate with the example of genus and differential, where knowledge of the parts is prior, but upon aggregation, the essence itself becomes posterior to them. The passage ends with a rebuttal of this denial, both by appeal to necessity and by inferential argument about parts being either external or internal.

Key Terms
حد
محدود
معرف
ماهية
جزء
هيئة اجتماعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A definition must introduce entirely new information about the parts of an essence.
Correction: The definer may merely arrange already known parts in a structured way, without informing of new parts.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the definer do if the parts of the essence are already known?
A) It introduces new parts that were previously unknown.
B) It arranges the known parts in an ordered set distinguished from others.
C) It proves that the parts are external to the essence.
D) It demonstrates that knowledge of the parts is identical to knowledge of the essence.

Key Takeaway
The definer of an essence need not teach new parts; it arranges known parts in a structured whole, as illustrated by the social form analogy.

Method of Learning
intermediate

Response to the Third Objection: Definition by Extrinsic Property
Sanusi responds to the objection that definition by extrinsic property requires prior knowledge of specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for a valid definition by extrinsic property and how the mind transitions from the necessitator to the necessary concomitant without prior knowledge.

Core Idea
Definition by extrinsic property requires a necessary concomitant that is also a mental necessitator.

Sanusi addresses the objection that definition by extrinsic property depends on prior knowledge of the specific property. He argues that the mind transfers from the necessitator to its consequent spontaneously, as illustrated by the body-space example. He then specifies the conditions for a suitable defining attribute: it must be a necessary concomitant in truth and a necessitator in conceptualization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the third objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note Sanusi's response and the example of the body occupying space.
2
Understand the mind's transition
Sanusi states that the mind moves from conceptualizing the necessitator (ملزوم) to its necessary concomitant (لازم) without prior knowledge of the implication.
3
Examine the condition for a defining attribute
The attribute must be a necessary concomitant in terms of truth (لازم بين الثبوت) and a necessitator in conceptualization (ملزوم بحسب التصور).
4
Reflect on the example
The body's occupation of a specific space is based on impossibility of being in two spaces; this illustrates how definition by extrinsic property works.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a continuous response to objections regarding the nature of definition (حد) and the definer (معرف). It addresses two specific objections (الثاني and الثالث) and includes an additional response from investigators. The author begins by clarifying a point about the definition being the sum of things each of which is a premise, explaining that the distinction between definition and defined is one of detail versus totality. Then, responding to the second objection, he argues that the definer of an essence does not necessarily have to inform of its parts, because the parts may already be known; instead, the definer brings them together in an ordered set distinguished from others. He explains that knowledge of the essence is not identical to knowledge of the parts as separate conceptualizations; rather, the parts must be observed collectively. He refutes claims that all parts of the essence are itself, using the analogy of social form (الهيئة الاجتماعية), which is the cause of the compound obtaining but not of any individual part. Addressing the third objection about definition by extrinsic property (التعريف بالخارج), he asserts that such definition does not require prior knowledge of the property's specificity; the mind transfers from the necessitator (ملزوم) to its mental consequent (لازم) spontaneously. He then discusses the conditions for a suitable defining attribute: it must be a necessary concomitant (لازم) in truth but also a necessitator (ملزوم) in conceptualization. Finally, some investigators answer the first objection (likely mentioned earlier) by denying that all parts of the essence are itself, using an argument about priority and posteriority. They illustrate with the example of genus and differential, where knowledge of the parts is prior, but upon aggregation, the essence itself becomes posterior to them. The passage ends with a rebuttal of this denial, both by appeal to necessity and by inferential argument about parts being either external or internal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Definition by extrinsic property requires prior knowledge of the specific property before it can be used to define.
Correction: The mind can transfer from the necessitator to the necessary concomitant without prior knowledge of the implication.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is required for an attribute to be suitable for defining a thing by extrinsic property?
A) It must be a necessary concomitant in truth only.
B) It must be a necessitator in conceptualization only.
C) It must be a necessary concomitant in truth and a necessitator in conceptualization.
D) It must be known prior to the definition.

Key Takeaway
A definition by extrinsic property is valid if the attribute is a necessary concomitant in truth and a necessitator in conceptualization, allowing the mind to transition without prior knowledge of the implication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your knowledge of Allah's attributes, how many of them do you truly understand through their necessary concomitants? Are you content with superficial acquaintance, or will you strive to grasp the deeper logical connections that Sanusi insists upon?
?
Consider a time you defined something by its extrinsic property without realizing the mental transition involved. How does this lesson reveal the need to verify that your definitions rest on proper necessitators? Will you examine your own definitions of key Islamic concepts?
?
Sanusi argues that definition by extrinsic property does not require prior knowledge of specificity. How does this principle affect your approach to learning? Are you willing to accept that you can gain knowledge through concepts you initially don't fully understand? This requires humility. Will you embrace it?

Method of Learning
advanced
6 min
The Investigators' Response to the First Objection: Parts of the Essence
Investigators respond to the first objection by denying that all parts are the essence itself, followed by a rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that not all parts of an essence are identical to the essence itself, based on priority and posteriority, and the rebuttal to that argument.

Core Idea
Parts of an essence are prior to the essence, so they cannot be identical to it.

Some investigators respond to the first objection by denying that all parts of the essence are identical to the essence. They argue that things each of which is prior to a thing cannot be the later thing itself. They illustrate with the example of genus and differential, where knowledge of parts precedes knowledge of the restricted genus. However, this denial is itself rebutted by appeals to necessity and inference. This lesson covers that partial exchange, though the full argument is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the investigators' argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the claim that not all parts are the essence itself, based on priority.
2
Understand the priority argument
Things that are prior to another thing cannot be identical to that later thing. Parts are prior to the compound essence.
3
Example with genus and differential
Knowledge of genus and differential is prior to knowledge of the restricted genus. The parts (genus and differential) are not the same as the composite.
4
Examine the rebuttal
The denial is rebutted by appealing to necessity and by an inference: if parts are not the essence, they must be either external (false) or internal (composing part of it), so they cannot be all the parts.
The rebuttal is incomplete in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجود الواحد، تارة جملة، وتارة شيئاً فشيئاً، ولم يرد في حل الإشكال على أن الحد مجموع الأمور التي كل واحد منها مقدم، ولا يجدي نفعاً لأن المحدود أيضاً كذلك، فلا بد في بيان المغايرة والسببية من أن يقال تلك الأمور من حيث الملاحظة تفصيلاً حداً وإجمالاً محدوداً وهو معنى كلامهم . وأما عن الثاني : فبأنا لا نسلم أن معرف الماهية يجب أن يعرف شيئاً من أجزائها، لجواز أن تكون الأجزاء معلومة، ونفتقر إلى حضورها مجموعة مترتبة ممتازة عما عداها ويكون ذلك بالمعرف وحاصله أن الماهية وإن كانت نفس الأجزاء بحسب الذات لكن لا يلزم أن يكون العلم بها هو العلم بالأجزاء، بمعنى التصورات المتعلقة بها، بل لا بد من ملاحظتها مجتمعة متميزة عن الأغيار، ويجوز أن تبقى الأجزاء مجهولة ويفيد المعرف بالذات، ويعود التغاير إلى الإجمال والتفصيل، كما في تعريف الماهية بأجزائها أو غيره، ويصح التعريف بالخارج على ما سيجيء وبما ذكرنا يندفع ما يقال إن أجزاء جميع الماهية نفسها، فكيف لا يكون العلم بها علماً بها؟ وأن معرف الشيء سبب المعرفته، أي حصوله في الذهن، فكيف لا يحصل شيئاً من أجزائه ..؟ وأن علة حصول الشيء لو لم تكن علة لشيء من أجزائه لجاز حصول كل جزء بدونه، فجاز حصول الكل بدونه فلم يكن علة ولنعتبر بالهيئة الاجتماعية فإنها علة الحصول المركب وليست علة لحصول شيء من أجزائه . وأما عن الثالث : فبأنا لا نسلم أن التعريف بالخارج يتوقف عن العلم باختصاصه بل على الاختصاص نفسه، فإن الذهن ينتقل من تصور الملزوم إلى تصور لازمه الذهني، وإن لم يتقدم العلم باللزوم، ولو سلم فيكفي في ذلك تصور الشيء بوجه، وتصور ما عداه إجمالاً، كما في اختصاص الجسم بهذا الحيز، وإن كان مبنياً على امتناع كونه في حيزين، واشتغال حيز بمتحيزين، وإلى هذا التسليم نظر من قال : الوصف الصالح لتعريف الشيء يجب أن يكون لازماً بين الثبوت لافراده، الانتفاء وبين عن جميع ما عداه، وينبغي أن يعلم أنه وإن كان لازماً بحسب الصدق لكن لا بد أن يكون ملزوماً بحسب التصور، وأجاب بعض المحققين عن الأول بمنع كون جميع أجزاء الماهية نفسها، بل جزم بأنه باطل تمسكاً بأن الأشياء التي كل واحد منها متقدم على الشيء يمتنع أن يكون نفس المتأخر . ثم قال : ويجوز أن يصير عند الاجتماع ماهيته هي المتأخرة، فتحصل معرفتها بها، كما أن العلم بالجنس والفصل وبالتركيب التقييدي متقدم على العلم بالجنس المقيد بالفصل وهي أجزاؤه، وبها يحصل العلم به، ورد المنع تارة بدعوى الضرورة، وتارة بالاستدلال بأن جميع أجزاء الشيء إن لم تكن نفسه فإما تكون خارجة عنه وهو ظاهر البطلان أو داخلة فيه فتركب الشيء منها ومن غيرها، فلا تكون هي جميع الأجزاء بل بعضها، وأيضاً لو كان الشيء
The single existent, sometimes as a whole, sometimes gradually, and did not intend in solving the problem that the definition (حد) is the sum of things each of which is a premise, and it does not avail because the defined (المحدود) is also like that, so in explaining the distinction and causality it must be said that those things from the perspective of detail are the definition and from the perspective of totality are the defined, and that is the meaning of their statement. As for the second [objection]: we do not concede that the definer (معرف) of the essence must inform something of its parts, because it is possible that the parts are known, and we need to have them present in an ordered set distinguished from others, and that is achieved by the definer; the gist is that the essence, even if it is the same parts in terms of essence, it does not necessarily follow that knowledge of it is knowledge of the parts, meaning the conceptualizations related to them, but rather they must be observed collectively distinguished from others, and it is possible that the parts remain unknown and the definer informs by essence, and the difference reverts to entirety and detail, as in defining the essence by its parts or other than them, and definition by extrinsic property (التعريف بالخارج) is valid as will come, and by what we have mentioned is repelled what is said: that all the parts of the essence are itself, so how is knowledge of them not knowledge of it? And that the definer of a thing is the cause of its being known, i.e. its mental existence, so how does it not obtain any of its parts? And that if the cause of obtaining a thing were not a cause for any of its parts, it would be permissible for each part to obtain without it, so it would be permissible for the whole to obtain without it, so it would not be a cause; and consider the social form (الهيئة الاجتماعية) for it is the cause of the compound obtaining and not the cause of any of its parts obtaining. As for the third [objection]: we do not concede that definition by extrinsic property depends on knowledge of its specificity, but on the specificity itself, for the mind transfers from the conceptualization of the necessitator to the conceptualization of its mental consequent, even if knowledge of the implication is not prior, and if conceded, then sufficient in that is the conceptualization of the thing in one respect and the conceptualization of what is other than it in general, as in the specificity of a body to this space, even if it is based on the impossibility of its being in two spaces and a space occupying two bodies, and to this concession referred the one who said: the attribute suitable for defining a thing must be a necessary concomitant (لازم) between affirmation of its individuals and negation from all others, and it should be known that even if it is necessary in terms of truth, it must be a necessitator (ملزوم) in terms of conceptualization. And some investigators answered the first [objection] by denying that all parts of the essence are itself, rather asserting it is false, adhering [to the argument] that things each of which is prior to a thing cannot be the later thing itself. Then he said: and it is possible that upon aggregation its essence becomes the later, so its knowledge is obtained by them, just as knowledge of genus and differential and by restrictive composition is prior to knowledge of the genus restricted by the differential, and they are its parts, and by them knowledge of it is obtained; and they rejected the denial sometimes by appealing to necessity and sometimes by inference that if all parts of a thing are not itself, then they are either external to it which is obviously false, or internal to it so the thing is composed of them and something else, so they are not all the parts but some of them; and also if the thing
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a continuous response to objections regarding the nature of definition (حد) and the definer (معرف). It addresses two specific objections (الثاني and الثالث) and includes an additional response from investigators. The author begins by clarifying a point about the definition being the sum of things each of which is a premise, explaining that the distinction between definition and defined is one of detail versus totality. Then, responding to the second objection, he argues that the definer of an essence does not necessarily have to inform of its parts, because the parts may already be known; instead, the definer brings them together in an ordered set distinguished from others. He explains that knowledge of the essence is not identical to knowledge of the parts as separate conceptualizations; rather, the parts must be observed collectively. He refutes claims that all parts of the essence are itself, using the analogy of social form (الهيئة الاجتماعية), which is the cause of the compound obtaining but not of any individual part. Addressing the third objection about definition by extrinsic property (التعريف بالخارج), he asserts that such definition does not require prior knowledge of the property's specificity; the mind transfers from the necessitator (ملزوم) to its mental consequent (لازم) spontaneously. He then discusses the conditions for a suitable defining attribute: it must be a necessary concomitant (لازم) in truth but also a necessitator (ملزوم) in conceptualization. Finally, some investigators answer the first objection (likely mentioned earlier) by denying that all parts of the essence are itself, using an argument about priority and posteriority. They illustrate with the example of genus and differential, where knowledge of the parts is prior, but upon aggregation, the essence itself becomes posterior to them. The passage ends with a rebuttal of this denial, both by appeal to necessity and by inferential argument about parts being either external or internal.

Key Terms
ماهية
جزء
جنس
فصل
تركيب تقييدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All parts of an essence are necessarily identical to the essence itself.
Correction: Some investigators argue that parts are prior to the essence and thus not identical, but this view is rebutted.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the investigators' denial that all parts of the essence are the essence itself?
A) The essence is simple and has no parts.
B) Parts are prior to the compound essence, so they cannot be identical.
C) The definer cannot inform of parts.
D) Definition by extrinsic property is preferable.

Key Takeaway
Investigators argued that parts are prior to the essence and thus not identical, but this was rebutted on grounds of necessity and the composition of parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that every aspect of your personality or identity is equally 'you'? This lesson shows that parts are prior to the whole. Which part of your self-concept might be prior to the rest, and how does that affect your understanding of yourself?
?
The rebuttal appeals to necessity. Is there something you consider self-evident about your own essence that might actually be a composition? What would it mean to find that a deeply held belief about yourself is not identical to who you truly are?
?
When you define yourself by certain attributes (e.g., profession, relationships, beliefs), are those attributes parts that are prior to your essence? How does this challenge your daily accountability before Allah? Will you commit to re-examining one such attribute today?

The Role of Necessary Propositions in Kalam
Taftazani justifies the inclusion of verifying necessary premises as a principle of kalam and lists six types of necessary judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why verifying necessary premises is a foundational principle of kalam and how Taftazani categorizes necessary judgments into six types.

Core Idea
Kalam must verify the necessary premises that underlie all theoretical reasoning.

Taftazani opens a methodological discussion by explaining that since all theoretical knowledge ultimately rests on necessary premises, the discipline of kalam must take on the task of verifying those premises. He then notes a practical point: scholars focused on necessary judgments rather than concepts, and they reduced them to six well-known categories. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Context: Theoretical sciences and necessity
Understand why theoretical sciences end in necessary premises and why this matters for kalam.
2
Principle: Verification and refutation
Learn why verifying necessary premises and refuting their deniers is a principle of kalam.
The goal is to ensure that premises claimed as necessary truly are necessary.
3
Distinction: Concepts vs. judgments
Note why scholars focused on necessary judgments rather than necessary concepts.
Concepts are said to reduce to axioms and observations, so they were not separately enumerated.
4
Enumeration: Six categories
Identify the six categories of necessary judgments: axioms, observations, innates, experiential, recurrent reports, and intuitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: لما كانت العلوم النظرية تنتهي إلى الضروريات جعلوا إثباتها والرد على منكريها من مبادىء الكلام ليعلم أن ما يجعل منتهى مقدمات القياس ويدعي كونه ضرورياً هل هو منها، ولم يشتغلوا بضبط التصورات الضرورية وكأنها ترجع إلى البديهات والمشاهدات وحصروا التصديقات الضرورية في ست البديهيات والمشاهدات والفطريات والمجربات والمتواترات والحدسيات.
I say: Since theoretical sciences end in the necessary [premises], they made their verification and the refutation of those who deny them among the principles of kalam, so that it may be known whether that which is made the terminus of the premises of a syllogism and claimed to be necessary is indeed among them. And they did not occupy themselves with controlling the necessary concepts, as if they revert to axioms and observations; and they confined the necessary judgments to six: axioms, observations, innates, experiential, recurrent reports, and intuitive.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a methodological justification. The author (Taftazani) explains why the study of necessary propositions is part of kalam. Theoretical sciences rely on necessary premises, so kalam must verify them (ithbat) and refute those who deny them. This is to ensure that what is used as the final premise of a syllogism, claimed to be necessary, truly is necessary. The scholars did not focus on controlling the necessary concepts (tasawwurat daruriyya), as they reduce to axioms (badihiyyat) and observations (mushahadat). Instead, they restricted the necessary judgments (tasdiqat daruriyya) to the six categories already enumerated: axioms, observations, innates, experiential, recurrent reports, and intuitive.

Key Terms

مبادىء الكلام
التصورات الضرورية
التصديقات الضرورية
بديهيات
مشاهدات
فطريات
مجربات
متواترات
حدسيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam only deals with theology, not the verification of basic premises of logic.
Correction: This passage shows that verifying necessary premises (the building blocks of all reasoning) is itself a principle of kalam.

Checkpoint
Why is verifying necessary premises a principle of kalam according to this passage?
A) Because all knowledge is theoretical
B) Because theoretical sciences end in necessary premises
C) Because the Mu'tazila denied them
D) Because they are easy to prove

Key Takeaway
The verification of necessary premises is a core principle of kalam, and necessary judgments are confined to six categories: axioms, observations, innates, experiential, recurrent reports, and intuitive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a claim about your faith that is presented as 'necessary' or 'obvious,' do you ever pause to verify whether it truly belongs to one of the six categories Taftazani lists? What is at stake if you don't?
?
Examine your own set of 'necessary beliefs'—those you take for granted in your practice of Islam. How many of them have you personally verified through observation, experience, or sound transmission? Which ones might you be holding without such verification?
?
The scholars of kalam did not shy from defending even the most basic premises. Is there a core belief you hold that you would be unable to defend if challenged? Will you commit to tracing it back to one of these six sources today?

The Nature of Tawatur Knowledge and the Two Premises
Al-Taftazani explains the intermediate nature of mutawatir knowledge citing Ghazali and concludes the dispute is verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge from tawatur is considered 'necessary' in a specific sense, and how al-Ghazali's analysis resolves a verbal dispute among scholars.

Core Idea
Mutawatir knowledge is necessary not because it has no premises, but because we are unaware of them.

Al-Taftazani, building on al-Ghazali, clarifies how knowledge through mass transmission (tawatur) occupies a middle ground between purely intuitive and acquired knowledge. The passage unpacks two meanings of 'necessary' and explains the two implicit premises that make the knowledge compelling. This analysis reveals that the classical disagreement is merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text slowly, then follow the literal translation. Notice the key terms like ضروري and واسطة.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يفتقر إلى الشعور بتوسط واسطة' – it is central.
2
Understand the two senses of 'necessary'
Al-Ghazali distinguishes: (1) necessary without any intermediary, (2) necessary with an intermediary but without conscious awareness. Identify where these are mentioned.
The example 'the existent is not non-existent' illustrates the second sense.
3
Identify the two premises
Find the two premises listed: the impossibility of a large diverse group conspiring on a lie, and their agreement on the report. Notice that these are not consciously arranged.
4
Grasp the conclusion that the dispute is verbal
The author states that the disagreement among scholars hinges on the definition of 'necessary'. Consider why this insight resolves the debate.
Think about how different definitions lead to different classifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واسطة لعدم افتقارها إلى الاكتساب الفكري، وبهذا يشعر كلام الإمام حجة الإسلام حيث قال: العلم الحاصل بالتواتر ضروري بمعنى أنه لا يحتاج إلى الشعور بتوسط واسطة مفضية إليه مع أن الواسطة حاضرة في الذهن، وليس ضرورياً بمعنى الحاصل من غير واسطة كما في قولنا: الموجود ليس بمعدوم فإنه لا بد فيه من حصول مقدمتين. إحداهما: أن هؤلاء مع كثرتهم واختلاف أحوالهم لا يجمعهم على الكذب جامع. الثانية: أنهم قد اتفقوا على الإخبار عن الواقعة لكنه لا يفتقر إلى ترتيب المقدمتين ولا إلى الشعور بتوسطهما ولا بإفضائهما إليه. وبهذا يظهر أن النزاع لفظي مبني على تفسير الضروري، أنه الذي لا يفتقر إلى واسطة أصلاً، أو الذي نجد أنفسنا مضطرين إليه.
A mediator due to its lack of need for intellectual acquisition, and this is indicated by the words of Imam Hujjat al-Islam where he said: Knowledge obtained through tawatur is necessary in the sense that it does not require awareness of an intermediary leading to it while the intermediary is present in the mind, and it is not necessary in the sense of being obtained without an intermediary, as in our statement: the existent is not non-existent, for it necessarily requires the occurrence of two premises. One of them: that these people, with their large number and differing circumstances, are not united upon falsehood. The second: that they have agreed upon reporting the event, but it does not require the arrangement of the two premises nor awareness of their mediation nor their leading to it. And by this it becomes clear that the dispute is verbal, based on the interpretation of 'necessary' as that which does not require any intermediary at all, or that which we find ourselves compelled to.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of knowledge obtained through tawatur (mass transmission). The author begins by clarifying that such knowledge is called 'necessary' (daruri) in a specific sense: it does not require an effort of intellectual acquisition because the premises that lead to it are already present in the mind, even if one is not consciously aware of them. He supports this by quoting al-Ghazali (Hujjat al-Islam), who distinguishes two meanings of 'necessary': one that arises without any intermediary, and one that arises with an intermediary but without conscious effort. The example given is the statement 'the existent is not non-existent' — this is not purely intuitive but depends on two premises implicitly. Those two premises are then listed: (1) that a large and diverse group cannot conspire on a lie, and (2) that they have all agreed on reporting the event. However, one does not need to consciously arrange these premises to reach the conclusion; it comes automatically. Therefore, the author concludes that the dispute about whether tawatur knowledge is necessary or acquired is merely verbal, depending on how one defines 'necessary'.

Key Terms
تواتر
ضروري
اكتسابي
مقدمتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that knowledge from tawatur is either purely necessary (no premises) or purely acquired (conscious deduction).
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows it is necessary in the sense that the premises are present but not consciously activated; thus it straddles the two categories.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's explanation cited in the passage, why is knowledge through tawatur called 'necessary'?
A) Because it is obtained without any premises whatsoever.
B) Because it does not require conscious awareness of the intermediate premises.
C) Because it is derived from sensory perception directly.
D) Because it is a result of logical deduction from first principles.

Key Takeaway
Tawatur knowledge is 'necessary' because its premises are automatically operative in the mind, making the dispute about its classification merely verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accept a core belief as absolutely certain—like the existence of God or the truth of revelation—do you ever stop to consider the hidden premises that brought you to that certainty? What might it mean if those premises were wrong?
?
Al-Ghazali shows that even 'the existent is not non-existent' depends on unspoken premises. What unspoken premises might underlie your own most deeply held convictions? Are you willing to examine them, or do you prefer not to know?
?
If the dispute over tawatur knowledge is merely verbal, what does that say about other disputes in theology? Are you sometimes arguing about mere definitions rather than substance? How can you train yourself to look beyond words to the reality they describe?

Transmitted Knowledge: Definition and Epistemological Status
Defines transmitted knowledge and discusses its necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how transmitted knowledge is defined as derived from mutawātirāt, why its certainty is disputed between necessary and acquired, and how a legal ruling is derived from prophetic reports.

Core Idea
Transmitted knowledge is disputed: necessary or acquired?

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid establishes the epistemological foundation for accepting transmitted reports (al-manqul) in theological arguments. It defines transmitted knowledge, flags a key dispute about its certainty, and illustrates the reasoning process with an example from Islamic law. Understanding this classification is crucial for grasping how scholars evaluate prophetic traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the definition and the mention of a dispute.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the definition and the dispute
Notice how transmitted knowledge is defined as derived from mutawātirāt, and that scholars disagree whether such knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī) or acquired (kasbī).
The dispute is about the certainty of the knowledge, not the report itself.
3
Examine the example and clarification
The example shows how an acquired legal ruling (burden of proof on claimant) is derived from two premises about the Prophet. The final sentence clarifies that calling a hadith mutawātir refers to the transmission of the wording, regardless of its original type (declarative or performative).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنقول كلام النبي هو المستفاد من القضية التي من قبيل المتواترات المتنازع في أنه ضروري أو غير ضروري والعلم بأن البينة تجب على المدعي كسبي مستفاد من ترتيب المقدمتين أعني أن هذا خبر النبي عليه السلام وكل ما هو خبر عن النبي عليه السلام فمضمونه حق، لما ثبت من صدقه بدلالة المعجزات وما يقال من أن هذا الحديث متواتر فمعناه أن الخبر كونه كلام النبي متواتر، سواء كان هو في نفسه خبراً أو إنشاءً.
The transmitted knowledge of the Prophet's speech is the knowledge derived from a proposition that belongs to the category of mutawātirāt (mass-transmitted reports), which is disputed as to whether it is necessary or not necessary. And the knowledge that the burden of proof is upon the claimant is acquired, derived from arranging the two premises, namely: that this is a report of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and everything that is a report from the Prophet (peace be upon him) has its content true, because his truthfulness is established by the evidence of miracles. And what is said that this ḥadīth is mutawātir, its meaning is that the report being the speech of the Prophet is mutawātir, whether it is in itself a report or a performative utterance.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It defines transmitted knowledge (al-manqūl) as that which is derived from a proposition belonging to the category of mutawātirāt (mass-transmitted reports). It then notes that there is a dispute regarding whether such knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī, self-evident) or acquired (kasbī, inferential). To illustrate, it gives an example: the knowledge that the burden of proof is on the claimant is acquired because it comes from arranging two premises: first, that this is a report from the Prophet, and second, that everything reported by him is true due to his proven truthfulness through miracles. It then clarifies that when it is said that a particular ḥadīth is mutawātir, what is meant is that the report itself (the utterance) is the speech of the Prophet and is transmitted by multiple independent chains, regardless of whether the original utterance is a khabar (declarative statement) or an inshā' (imperative or performative speech act). This establishes the epistemological basis for accepting transmitted reports in kalam arguments.

Key Terms
المنقول
متواترات
ضروري
كسبي
خبر
إنشاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mutawātir knowledge is always necessary (self-evident) and never requires inference.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that there is a dispute over whether transmitted knowledge is necessary or acquired, and it provides an example of such knowledge being acquired through inference.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the knowledge that the burden of proof is upon the claimant is acquired (kasbī) because it is derived from:
A) Direct sensory experience
B) Arranging two premises about the Prophet's report
C) Intuitive self-evident reasoning
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Transmitted knowledge is based on mutawātir reports, but scholars disagree whether such knowledge is self-evident or inferential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a hadith, do you automatically assume its truth is self-evident, or do you examine the chain of transmission and the proofs for its authenticity? How does your assumption affect your certainty?
?
The lesson shows that even a simple legal ruling like 'the burden of proof is on the claimant' requires piecing together two premises. In your own faith, are there beliefs you hold that you have never examined the premises for? What would it take for you to verify them?
?
If a scholar told you that a certain belief is 'necessary' (ḍarūrī), would you accept it without question, or would you ask how it qualifies as necessary? How would you respond if someone challenged a belief you considered self-evident?

Consensus on Sense and Intuition as Foundations of Knowledge
Explains consensus on sense and intuition and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq that sense perceptions and intuitive axioms are primary foundations of knowledge, and how the author defends this against various objections, particularly clarifying the role of the senses and intellect.

Core Idea
Sense and intuition are the bedrock of all certainty, but their judgments require intellectual scrutiny.

This lesson delves into a foundational debate in Islamic epistemology: Are sense perceptions and intuitive axioms the primary sources of certain knowledge? The author affirms the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq, then engages with objections from skeptics and rationalists, clarifying the nuanced roles of the senses and intellect. You will see how the author rebuts the claim that philosophers deny certainty to sense perceptions by showing their reliance on them, and how he explains the need for a higher judge (the intellect) to ensure certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus statement
Read the opening sentence which states the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq that sense perceptions and intuitive axioms are primary principles for proof.
This is the thesis the author defends.
2
Note the three groups of objectors
The passage lists three groups: those who attack sense perceptions, those who attack intuitive axioms, and those who attack both. Each has specious arguments.
The author does not detail their arguments here but references Imam al-Razi's elaboration.
3
Follow the rebuttal of the attribution to philosophers
The author refutes the claim that philosophers deny certainty to sense perceptions by showing that their sciences are built on sense perceptions and that they acknowledge sense perception as the source of empirical, mutawātir, and intuitive knowledge.
This is a key defense of the philosophers.
4
Understand the discussion on conditions and the intellect as judge
The passage explains that the mind's certainty from senses depends on conditions, leading to sciences like optics. It then addresses the argument that sense is not a judge; the intellect judges everything.
The author clarifies that error can occur in both sensed and intelligible matters, and the intellect is the ultimate arbiter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول قد ثبت اتفاق أهل الحق على أن الحسيات والبديهيات مبادىء أول لما يقوم حجة على الغير، وأنكر ذلك جماعة فمنهم من قدح في الحسيات وحصر المبادىء الأول في البديهيات ومنهم من عكس، ومنهم من قدح فيهما جميعاً، ولكل من الفرق شبه، وقد أطنب الإمام فيها بتكثير الأمثلة، ونسب القول بعدم كون الحسيات من اليقينيات إلى أكابر الفلاسفة، ورد بأن علومهم اليقينية مبنية عليها، والمبادىء الضرورية مستندة إليها، على ما صرحوا بأن مبادىء المجربات والمتواترات والحدسيات هي الإحساس بالجزئيات، وأن الأوليات يكتسبها الصبيان باستعداد يحصل لعقولهم من الإحساس بالجزئيات، فكيف ينسب إليهم القول بأنها ليست يقينية؟ واعتذر بأن المراد أن جزم العقل بالأحكام المأخوذة من الحس قد تتوقف على شرائط، ربما لا يعلم ماهي. ومتى حصلت؟ وكيف حصلت؟ فلذلك جعلوا لبيان مواضع الغلط في المحسوسات، وأن أي أحكامها تكون يقينية، وأيها تكون غير يقينية، صناعة المناظر كما جعلوا لبيان ذلك في المعقولات صناعة سوفسطيا، وما ذكر في تلخيص المحصل من أنه لا حكم للحس لأنه ليس من شأنه التأليف الحكمي، بل الإدراك فقط، وإنما الحكم للعقل، ليس رداً لكلام الإمام بالمناقشة في أن الحاكم هو الحس أو العقل بواسطته، بل لما رتب عليه من المقصود حيث قال: فالمحسوس من حيث إنه محسوس لا يوصف بكونه يقينياً أو غير يقيني، وإنما يوصف به من حيث مقارنته لحكم العقل، وحينئذ يصير المعنى أن أحكام العقل في المحسوسات ليست بيقينية لما قد يقع فيها من الغلط، وهذا لا يختص بالمحسوسات، لأن المعقولات الصرفة أيضاً قد يقع فيها الغلط، ولا تصح نسبته إلى الحكماء لتصريحهم بخلاف ذلك. نعم لما ذكر الإمام أنه ثبت بما ذكر من الشبه أن حكم الحس قد يكون غلطاً فلا بد من حاكم آخر فوقه يميز صوابه عن خطئه، فلا يكون الحس هو الحاكم الأول، رده بأن الحس ليس بحاكم أصلاً، بل الحاكم في الكل هو العقل؛ وأما اشتغاله ببيان أسباب الغلط فيما أورده الإمام من الصور فقد اعترف بأنه تنبيه لمن يثق أو يعترف بالوقوف على الأوليات والمحسوسات ببيان التقصي عن مضايق مواضع الغلط، ثم إحالة تصويب
I say: It is established by the consensus of the People of Truth (Ahl al-Ḥaqq) that sense perceptions (al-ḥissiyyāt) and intuitive axioms (al-badīhiyyāt) are primary principles (mabādi' uwwal) for what constitutes a proof against others. A group denied this; among them are those who attacked sense perceptions and limited the primary principles to intuitive axioms; among them are those who did the opposite; and among them are those who attacked both. Each of these groups has specious arguments. The Imām (al-Rāzī) elaborated at length with many examples, and attributed the view that sense perceptions are not among certainties (yaqīniyyāt) to the greatest philosophers. He rebutted [this attribution] by saying that their certain sciences are built upon them, and the necessary principles are based on them, given that they explicitly stated that the principles of experimental, mutawātir, and intuitive knowledge are sense perception of particulars, and that the primary axioms are acquired by children through a preparedness that arises in their minds from sense perception of particulars. So how can the view that they are not certain be attributed to them? He excused it by saying that what is meant is that the mind's certainty regarding judgments taken from the senses may depend on conditions whose nature may not be known, when they occur, or how they occur. Therefore, they established the science of optics (ʿilm al-manāẓir) to explain the places of error in sensed objects and which of their judgments are certain and which are not certain, just as they established the art of sophistry (ṣināʿat sūfisṭiyā) for explaining that in intelligibles. And what was mentioned in Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal—that there is no judgment of the senses because it is not in their nature to form compound judgments, but only perception, and that judgment belongs solely to the intellect—is not a refutation of the Imām's words by disputing whether the judge is the sense or the intellect through its mediation, but rather due to what he built upon it of the intention, where he said: 'The sensed object, insofar as it is sensed, is not described as certain or uncertain; rather it is described as such by virtue of its correlation with the intellect's judgment. And then the meaning becomes that the intellect's judgments concerning sensed objects are not certain because of the error that may occur in them.' And this is not specific to sensed objects, because pure intelligibles also may contain error. And it is not correct to attribute this to the philosophers (al-ḥukamā') due to their explicit statements to the contrary. Indeed, when the Imām mentioned that it is established by the mentioned specious arguments that the judgment of the senses may be erroneous, so there must be another judge above it to distinguish its correct from its incorrect, so the sense is not the primary judge, he refuted it by saying that the sense is not a judge at all; rather the judge in everything is the intellect. As for his engagement in explaining the causes of error in the examples presented by the Imām, he acknowledged that it is a warning for whoever trusts or admits to stopping at the primary axioms and sensed objects, by explaining the scrutiny of narrow places of error, then referring correction.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative discussion about the epistemological foundations. It begins by stating the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth) that sensory perceptions (al-ḥissiyyāt) and intuitive axioms (al-badīhiyyāt) are primary principles for establishing proofs. Then it notes that some groups denied this, attacking either sense perceptions, intuition, or both, and each has specious arguments. The author mentions that Imam al-Razi extensively documented this and attributed to the greatest philosophers the view that sense perceptions are not certain. The author then rebuts this attribution by pointing out that the philosophers themselves base their certain sciences on sense perception; they state that the principles of empirical, mutawātir, and intuitive knowledge come from sense perception of particulars, and even primary axioms are acquired by children through sense perception. Thus, they cannot deny certainty to sense perceptions. The author explains that what the philosophers meant is that the mind's certainty from senses depends on certain conditions, so they established the science of optics to determine when judgments are certain or not, just as they established sophistry for intelligibles. Next, the passage discusses the view from Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal that the senses do not judge but only perceive, and judgment belongs to the intellect. The author clarifies that this is not a refutation of al-Razi but a clarification that the sensed object as such is not described as certain; only when combined with the intellect's judgment can it be certain. The author then notes that this issue is not exclusive to sensed objects; pure intelligibles can also contain error. He rejects attributing the negation of certainty to philosophers. Finally, the passage recounts al-Razi's argument that since sense judgments can be erroneous, there must be a higher judge (the intellect) to distinguish correct from incorrect. The response is that the sense is not a judge at all; the intellect judges everything. The passage ends abruptly while discussing the explanation of error in examples.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers deny all certainty from sense perceptions.
Correction: The philosophers affirm that sense perceptions are the basis for certain sciences, but they acknowledge that certainty depends on conditions and is subject to error, which is why they developed sciences like optics to analyze it.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that philosophers deny certainty to sense perceptions?
A) By quoting their works directly
B) By showing that philosophers base their certain sciences on sense perceptions
C) By stating that philosophers rely on intuition instead
D) By arguing that sense perception is never certain

Key Takeaway
Even though sense perceptions can be erroneous, they are essential foundations for knowledge when refined by the intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your senses every day to form beliefs about the world. But this lesson warns that sense judgments can be mistaken, and the intellect must judge. When was the last time you actually paused to question whether a 'certain' perception of yours—about a person, a situation, or even a religious text—was really correct, or simply a hasty judgment? What would it take for you to develop that rigorous habit of scrutinizing your own certainties?
?
The philosophers themselves built their sciences on sense perception, yet they also acknowledged conditions for error. In your own journey of seeking knowledge, do you tend to trust your intellect too much, dismissing the evidence of your senses? Or do you trust your senses too much, neglecting the intellectual scrutiny needed? Where in your life right now is one of these imbalances causing you to hold onto an unfounded certainty? Commit to one concrete step to correct that imbalance today.
?
This passage shows that even children acquire primary axioms through sense perception. How much of your foundational beliefs about God, the world, and yourself were formed through uncritical sensory experiences and cultural conditioning? Have you ever subjected those foundational beliefs to the same intellectual scrutiny that the lesson demands—examining whether they are truly certain or merely familiar? If you did, would you find yourself correcting some of them? Will you do that now?

Objection and Response on the Definition of Nazar
Objection and response on defining nazar with 'ghalabat al-dhan'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against defining nazar as 'seeking knowledge or overwhelming conjecture' and how scholars responded to these objections.

Core Idea
Defining speculative thought requires careful attention to its specific properties and the logic of definitions.

In this passage, we encounter a scholarly debate about the proper definition of nazar. The definition proposed by Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani is challenged on the grounds that it does not cover all cases. Al-Amidi and others offer responses, leading to a rich discussion about the nature of definitions and the role of 'overwhelming conjecture' in careful thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the argument flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the back-and-forth structure of objection and response.
2
Identify the two main objections
The passage presents two objections: first, that the definition does not cover seeking 'original conjecture'; second, that restricting to some specific properties is problematic. Find each and see how they are answered.
The first objection is answered by al-Amidi; the second involves a critique from al-Mawaqif.
3
Reflect on the definitional issues
Consider why the author finds al-Amidi's final response unclear. Think about the logic of using 'or' in definitions and the concept of comprehensive specific properties.
This connects to the learning objective about the logic of definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطابق، ليلزم طلب الجهل وفي عبارة القاضي أبي بكر (1) علم أو غلبة ظن، واعترض بأنه لا يتناول ما يطلب به أصل الظن، وأجاب الآمدي بأن كلاً طلب العلم، وطلب الظن، وطلب غلبته، وخاصة للنظر . ولا خلل في الاقتصار على بعض الخواص ورده في المواقف) (2) من الثلاثة بأن هذا إنما يكون في الخاصة (3) الشاملة، وظاهر أن شيئاً منها ليست كذلك، ولهذا لم يجز الاقتصار على قولنا يطلب به علم لخروج ما يطلب به ظن بل وجب في تعريف الشيء بالخواص التي لا يشمل كل منها إلا بعض أقسامه أن يذكر الجميع بطريق التقسيم تحصيلاً لخاصة شاملة لكل فرد هي كونه على أحد الأوصاف، وتقع كلمة « أو » لبيان أقسام المحدود لا للإبهام والترديد لينافي التحديد، فأجاب بأن الظن هو المعبر عنه بغلبة الظن، لأن الرجحان مأخوذ في حقيقته، إذ هي الاعتقاد الراجح وهذا عذر غير واضح لأن اعتبار رجحان الحكم في حقيقته لا يصلح مصححاً، أو باعثاً على التعبير عنه برجحان الظن، اللهم إلا أن يريد أن إضافة الغلبة إليه للاختصاص، أي الرجحان المعتبر في الظن، وليست من إضافة المصدر إلى الفاعل، بمعنى كون الظن غالباً راجحاً . وقد يقال إن كلاً من الثلاث خاصة شاملة، إذ ليس المراد طلب العلم أو الظن بالفعل بل أن يكون الفكر بهذه الحيثية وذلك بأن يكون المعقولات لتحصيل مبادىء المطلوب، فالفكر الذي يطلب به العلم هو الذي يطلب به الظن (1) أو غلبته، فلا يمتنع الاقتصار.
Corresponding, to necessitate seeking ignorance. And in the statement of Qadi Abu Bakr: 'knowledge or overwhelming conjecture'. And it was objected that it does not include what seeks the original conjecture. And al-Amidi answered that each is seeking knowledge, seeking conjecture, and seeking its overwhelmingness, and it is a specific property for speculative thought. There is no defect in restricting to some specific properties, and its refutation in al-Mawaqif: that this only applies to the comprehensive specific property, and it is apparent that none of them is such, and therefore it is not permissible to restrict to our saying 'seeking by it knowledge' because it excludes what seeks by it conjecture. Rather, in defining a thing by specific properties each of which includes only some of its parts, it is necessary to mention all of them by way of division to obtain a comprehensive specific property for every individual, namely being upon one of the descriptions. And the word 'or' occurs to explain the divisions of the defined term, not for obscurity and hesitation which would contradict definition. So he answered that conjecture is that which is expressed as 'overwhelming conjecture', because preponderance is taken into its reality, since it is the preponderant belief. This excuse is unclear because considering the preponderance of the judgment in its reality is not valid to be a corrector or motivator for expressing it by preponderance of conjecture, unless he means that the addition of overwhelmingness to it is for specification, i.e., the preponderance considered in conjecture, and it is not an addition of the verbal noun to the agent in the sense of conjecture being overwhelming and preponderant. And it may be said that each of the three is a comprehensive specific property, since what is meant is not seeking knowledge or conjecture in actuality, but rather that the thought be in this respect, i.e., by considering universals for obtaining premises of what is sought. Thus the thought by which knowledge is sought is the same as that by which conjecture or its overwhelmingness is sought, so restriction is not impossible.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response regarding the definition of speculative thought (al-nazar). It begins by citing al-Baqillani's definition: 'seeking knowledge or overwhelming conjecture'. An objector argues that this does not cover seeking 'original conjecture' (al-dhan itself). Al-Amidi answers that the definition implicitly covers seeking knowledge, seeking conjecture, and seeking its overwhelmingness, as each is a specific property of nazar. A second objection is raised: restricting the definition to some specific properties is problematic because the properties listed are not comprehensive. The response explains that when each specific property covers only part of the defined, all must be listed by division to achieve a comprehensive property. The word 'or' is explained as indicating divisions, not ambiguity. The author then examines the response that conjecture is expressed as 'overwhelming conjecture' because preponderance is part of its reality, but finds this unclear unless 'overwhelming' is for specification. Finally, it is argued that each of the three is a comprehensive specific property because nazar seeks either knowledge or conjecture in terms of the thought process itself, not the result. Thus the restriction is acceptable.

Key Terms
الناظر
الظن
غلبة الظن
العلم
القاضي أبو بكر
الآمدي
الخاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'overwhelming conjecture' is redundant because conjecture already implies preponderance.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'overwhelming' may be added for specificity, indicating that the preponderance is a considered property, not that conjecture itself is always overwhelming. The debate shows that both terms have distinct roles in the definition.

Checkpoint
What was the objection to the definition of nazar as 'seeking knowledge or overwhelming conjecture'?
A) It does not cover seeking original conjecture.
B) It is too broad.
C) It is too narrow.
D) It is circular.

Key Takeaway
The definition of nazar must be comprehensive; objections help refine the understanding of its specific properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief, do you ever examine it with the same logical precision as this discussion demands—identifying its specific properties and ensuring your definition is comprehensive? Or do you settle for vague understandings? Commit to analyzing one of your core beliefs this week by listing its specific properties.
?
This lesson shows that even seasoned scholars debated definitions rigorously. When you encounter an objection to your faith or understanding, do you engage with its logical structure or dismiss it emotionally? Will you take the time to trace the argument step by step as was done here, using the exact statements?
?
The final resolution hinges on seeing that the thought process itself is the same regardless of outcome. Are there areas in your spiritual life where you focus on outcomes (e.g., certain results) rather than the sincerity and method of your seeking? How might shifting focus to the quality of your intellectual effort change your approach to learning and worship?

Division of Nazar into Correct and Incorrect
Sanusi divides nazar into correct/incorrect based on matter and form, then details conditions for soundness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion for correct nazar based on the soundness of its matter and form, and the specific conditions for soundness in definers and proofs.

Core Idea
Correct nazar requires both sound matter and sound form; one without the other fails.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid establishes the foundational classification of rational reflection (nazar) into correct and incorrect. Before we can judge any theological argument, we need to know what makes an argument valid. Sanusi lays out the dual criterion of matter and form, then specifies what soundness means for definers (mu'arif) and proofs (dalil). This prepares the student to evaluate arguments in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main division
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview. Notice the opening sentence which directly gives the criterion: if matter and form are sound, nazar is correct; otherwise it is incorrect.
This division is the central thesis of the passage.
2
Understand matter and form in nazar
The passage explains that matter refers to the sciences or premises used, and form is the logical arrangement. It also clarifies a technical debate about whether the arrangement itself is the form or the resulting configuration.
This distinction is crucial for later evaluation of arguments.
3
Examine conditions for soundness in definers (mu'arif)
List the conditions for a correct definer: the genus must be truly the genus, the differentia truly the differentia, and any property must be comprehensive and necessary. Also note the requirement for complete definitions to use proximate genus and differentia.
These conditions ensure the definer correctly conveys the essence.
4
Examine conditions for soundness in proofs (dalil)
For proofs, the premises must be appropriate to the desired conclusion and must be true, either certainty, probability, or hypothesis.
This covers both demonstrative and dialectical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
تقسيم النظر إلى صحيح وفاسد
قوله : (المبحث الثاني: النظر إن صحت مادته وصورته فصحيح وإلا ففاسد) سواء جعلناه نفس الترتيب أو الحركة المفضية إليه يستدعي علوماً مرتبـة على هيئة مخصوصة، يسمى الموصل منها إلى التصور معرفاً، وإلى التصديق دليلا، وتكون العلوم أي الأمور الحاضرة مادة لذلك الموصل، والهيئة المحصلة صورة له، وقد يضافان إلى النظر لهذه الملابسة، أو اطلاقا للفكر والنظر على العلوم المرتبة كما في عبارة الإمام، وهذا معنى كلام المواقف : أن لكل ترتيب مادة وصورة ثم الشائع في عبارة البعض أن الصورة هي ذلك الترتيب، إلا أن المحققين على أن الترتيب هو أن يكون لبعض أجزاء ذلك المجموع عند البعض وضع ما، أو جعلها بهذه الحيثية والصورة هي الهيئة العارضة للأجزاء بعد الترتيب بسببها يقال لها إنها واحدة واتفقوا على أنه إن صحت المادة والصورة، فالنظر صحيح يؤدي إلى المطلوب وإلا ففاسد لا يؤدي إليه، وصحة المادة في المعرف أن يكون المذكور في معرض الجنس جنساً للماهية، وفي معرض الفصل فصلا لها وفي معرض الخاصة خاصة شاملة لازمة وأن يكون المذكور في الحد التام الجنس والفصل القريبين إلى غير ذلك من الشرائط، وفي الدليل أن تكون المقدمات مناسبة للمطلوب، صادقة قطعاً أو ظناً أو فرضاً
The second investigation: Division of nazar into correct and incorrect. His saying: (The second investigation: If its matter and form are sound, then nazar is correct; otherwise it is incorrect.) Whether we make nazar the arrangement itself or the movement leading to it, it requires sciences arranged in a specific manner. That which leads to tasawwur is called mu'arif, and that which leads to tasdiq is called dalil. The sciences, i.e., the present matters, are the matter for that connector, and the resulting configuration is its form. They may be attributed to nazar due to this connection, or by applying fikr and nazar to the arranged sciences, as in the expression of the Imam. This is the meaning of al-Mawaqif's statement: that every arrangement has a matter and a form. Then it is common in the expression of some that the form is that arrangement, but the verifiers hold that the arrangement is that some parts of that whole have a certain position relative to others, or are placed in that respect, and the form is the configuration that accrues to the parts after arrangement due to which it is said that they are one. And they agreed that if the matter and form are sound, then nazar is correct and leads to the desired end; otherwise it is incorrect and does not lead to it. The soundness of the matter in the mu'arif is that what is mentioned in the context of genus be the genus of the essence, and in the context of differentia be the differentia for it, and in the context of property be a comprehensive necessary property, and that what is mentioned in the complete definition be the proximate genus and differentia, and so on from other conditions. And in the dalil, the premises must be appropriate to the desired end, certain or probable or hypothetical truth.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a classification of nazar (rational reflection) into correct (sahih) and incorrect (fasid). It begins by stating the criterion: nazar is correct if both its matter (madda) and form (sura) are sound; otherwise it is incorrect. The author then clarifies that whether we define nazar as the arrangement itself or as the mental movement that produces the arrangement, it necessarily involves sciences arranged in a specific manner. The part that yields a concept (tasawwur) is called a mu'arif (definer), while the part that yields assent (tasdiq) is called a dalil (proof). The sciences, i.e., the immediate objects of knowledge, constitute the matter for that connecting arrangement, and the resulting configuration (hay'a) is its form. The terms 'matter' and 'form' are applied to nazar either because of this close connection or by extension, as when the Imam uses 'fikr' and 'nazar' to refer to the arranged sciences themselves. The passage then references al-Mawaqif, stating that every arrangement has a matter and a form. A subtle point follows: some scholars say the form is simply the arrangement, but the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) distinguish the arrangement (tartib) — which is the relative positioning of parts — from the form (sura), which is the configuration that results from the arrangement and makes the parts a unified whole. All agree that if both matter and form are sound, nazar leads to the desired conclusion; if not, it fails. Finally, the passage details the conditions for soundness. For the mu'arif, the genus, differentia, and property must be correctly stated: the genus must truly be the genus of the essence (mahiyya), the differentia must be the differentia, and the property (khassa) must be inclusive and necessary (lazima). In a complete definition (hadd tam), the proximate genus and differentia must be used, along with other standard conditions. For the dalil, the premises must be appropriate to the desired conclusion, and they must be true — either with certainty (qat'an), probability (zannan), or as a hypothesis (fardan) for dialectical purposes. This classification establishes the criteria by which kalam arguments are evaluated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only the matter (content) of an argument matters for its correctness.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that both matter and form must be sound; even true premises can lead to error if the logical arrangement is flawed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion for nazar to be considered correct (sahih)?
A) It must lead to a novel conclusion.
B) It must be long and detailed.
C) Its matter and form must both be sound.
D) It must be based on Qur'anic verses.

Key Takeaway
The correctness of rational reflection depends equally on the soundness of its constituent material and the logical arrangement that ties them together.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your beliefs about Allah, do you ensure that both the matter (what you know) and the form (how you arrange it) are sound? Have you ever been convinced by an argument only because its premises sounded true, but its structure was flawed? How will you now check both before accepting a conclusion?
?
The passage insists that premises for a proof must be true. Are you honest with yourself about the premises of your own arguments? Do you sometimes assume things without verifying them? What would change if you treated your thought process with this rigorous standard every day?
?
The verifiers distinguish arrangement from form—the unity that results. In your pursuit of Islamic knowledge, do you merely collect information (arrangement) or do you seek a unified understanding (form)? Which do you prioritize, and how will you adjust to build a coherent worldview from what you learn?

The Division of Nazar into Valid/Invalid and Clear/Unclear
Al-Taftazani divides nazar into valid/invalid and clear/unclear metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani divides nazar into valid/invalid and clear/unclear, and why this division is considered metaphorical.

Core Idea
Nazar's validity and clarity depend on its matter and form, a metaphorical distinction that illuminates the logical structure.

Al-Taftazani builds on the previous discussion of definition and proof to classify nazar itself. He presents two binary divisions: sound vs. unsound and clear vs. obscure. However, he warns that this division is metaphorical, not literal, because it relies on the material and formal aspects of reasoning. This lesson unpacks that classification and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Taftazani uses the phrase 'metaphorical division' (tajawuz).
2
Identify the two divisions
The passage presents two divisions of nazar: sahih/fasid (valid/invalid) and jali/khafi (clear/unclear). Find the basis for each division.
Both are based on consideration of matter (madda) and form (sura).
3
Understand why the division is metaphorical
The passage states that the components of definition and proof may be necessary or theoretical, varying in clarity. Thus validity and clarity are not fixed but relative, making the division metaphorical. Also note the clarification about al-Mawaqif.
The metaphor arises because the division depends on the varying degrees of clarity in the components.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحسب المطالب على ما بين في الصناعات الخمس، وصحة الصورة في المعرف أن يقدم الأعم فيقيد بالفصل أو الخاصة بحيث تحصل صورة وجدانية موازية أو مميزة لصورة المطلوب، وفي الدليل أن يكون على الشرائط المعتبرة في الانتاج على ما فصل في أبواب القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل من المنطق، فظهر أن في تقسيمه النظر إلى الصحيح والفاسد باعتبار المادة والصورة تجوزاً فلا يبعد تقسيم إلى الجلي والخفي بهذا الاعتبار أيضاً فإن أجزاء كل من المعرف والدليل قد تكون ضرورية تتفاوت في الجلاء والخفاء وقد تكون نظرية تنتهي إلى الضروري بوسائط أقل أو أكثر وكذا الصورة القياسية للأشكال، وعبارة المواقف ربما توهم اختصاص التجوز بانقسام النظر إلى الجلي والخفي واختصاصه بالدليل دون المعرف وابتناء انقسام النظر إلى الصحيح والفاسد باعتبار المادة والصورة على تفسيره بالترتيب.
According to the demands as explained in the five arts, and the correctness of form in a definition is that the more general be presented then restricted by a differentia or a special property such that a mental image is obtained parallel to or distinguishing the image of the definiendum. And in a proof, it must meet the conditions considered for producing knowledge as detailed in the chapters of syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil) from logic. Thus it appears that dividing nazar into valid and invalid by consideration of matter and form is metaphorical, so it is not unlikely to divide it into clear and unclear by this consideration as well. For the parts of both the definition and the proof may be necessary, varying in clarity and obscurity, and may be theoretical, ending in the necessary through fewer or more intermediaries, and likewise the syllogistic form for the figures. And the expression of al-Mawaqif may suggest that the metaphorical division is specific to the division of nazar into clear and unclear, and specific to the proof without the definition, and that the division of nazar into valid and invalid by consideration of matter and form is based on its interpretation in order.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for correctness in definition and proof, then classifies nazar into valid/invalid and clear/unclear. The author explains that the division of nazar into sahih (valid) and fasid (invalid) by consideration of matter and form is metaphorical (tajawuz). Similarly, the division into jali (clear) and khafi (unclear) is also possible by this consideration because the components of definition and proof can vary in clarity. The author also notes that al-Mawaqif might be misinterpreted as limiting the metaphorical division to the clear/unclear division and to proof only, but the correct view is that it applies to both.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division of nazar into valid and invalid is literal, not metaphorical.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that this division is metaphorical (tajawuz) because it relies on the varying clarity of components.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the division of nazar into sahih and fasid considered metaphorical?
