The First Proof: Knowledge from Nazar as Necessary or Theoretical
Author examines whether knowledge from nazar is necessary or theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a classic kalam proof that correct nazar produces self-evident knowledge, and how the author defends against objections of human error and infinite regress.

Core Idea
Correct nazar yields self-evident knowledge; objections of error and regress are deflected.

The author begins the first proof by examining whether the knowledge that a belief resulting from nazar is itself knowledge is necessary or theoretical. Two objections arise: if necessary, error after nazar seems to contradict; if theoretical, an infinite regress threatens. The response shows that both horns can be escaped by restricting nazar to correct nazar, which is self-sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the tight logical structure of a dilemma and two responses.
The passage is cut off at the end but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The author presents two possibilities: the knowledge is either necessary or theoretical. For each, an objection is raised. Identify what happens if necessary (error would be impossible) and if theoretical (infinite regress).
The objection to the necessary horn is that people err after nazar. The objection to the theoretical horn is the need for another nazar.
3
Analyze the author's dual response
The author says 'we choose that it is necessary' and denies that error appears from correct nazar. Alternatively, 'we choose that it is theoretical' but denies it needs another nazar because correct nazar itself suffices. Notice he does not take a fixed position but defends both options.
This shows the argument is dialectical; the author is showing that the objection fails regardless of which horn is chosen.
4
Reflect on the role of 'correct nazar'
The key move is restricting discussion to 'correct nazar' (النظر الصحيح). This disqualifies faulty reasoning and explains why errors occur: they are not from correct nazar.
This is a common kalam strategy: the object of inquiry is the ideal, not the actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول : أن العلم بان الاعتقاد الحاصل عقيب النظر علم إن كان ضرورياً لم يظهر أي لم يقع عقيب النظر خلاف ذلك، أو لم يظهر بعد هذا خلاف ذلك، لامتناع أن يقع أو يظهر خلاف الضروري، واللازم باطل، لأن كثيراً من الناس لا يحصل عقيب نظرهم إلا الجهل وكثيراً ما ينكشف للناظر خلاف ما حصل من نظره ويظهر خطؤه، ولذلك تنتقل المذاهب، وإن كان نظرياً افتقر إلى نظر آخر يفيد العلم بأنه علم ويتسلسل ورد بأنا نختار أنه ضروري، ولا نسلم ظهور الخلاف من هذا النظر أو بعده إذ الكلام في النظر الصحيح، ولازم الحق حق قطعاً، أو نختار أنه نظري ولا نسلم افتقاره إلى نظر آخر، فإن النظر الصحيح
The first point: that the knowledge that the belief obtained after nazar is knowledge: if it is necessary, then contrary to it would not appear—i.e., it does not occur after nazar, or it does not appear after this—because it is impossible for a contrary of the necessary to occur or appear, and the consequent is false, because many people obtain only ignorance after their nazar and often the nazar discovers contrary to what was obtained and its error appears, hence schools of thought change. And if it is theoretical, it would need another nazar to yield the knowledge that it is knowledge, leading to infinite regress. It is responded: we choose that it is necessary, and we do not grant the appearance of contrary from this nazar or after it, for the discussion is about correct nazar, and the consequent of truth is certainly true; or we choose that it is theoretical, and we do not grant its need for another nazar, for correct nazar...
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents the first of several proofs (الوجه الأول). It considers whether the knowledge that the belief resulting from nazar is itself knowledge is necessary or theoretical. If it is necessary, the objection says that contrary to it would not appear, yet we see people erring after nazar. The response chooses the option that it is necessary and denies that contrary appears from correct nazar. If it is theoretical, the objection says it would require an infinite regress. The response chooses the option that it is theoretical but denies the need for another nazar, arguing that correct nazar itself suffices. The passage is cut off mid-sentence, but the author seems to be developing a defense of the self-evident nature of the knowledge from nazar.

Key Terms
الاعتقاد
النظر
الضروري
النظري
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because many people err after engaging in nazar, nazar cannot produce knowledge.
Correction: The author clarifies that the discussion concerns 'correct nazar' (النظر الصحيح). Errors arise from faulty reasoning, not from correct nazar. Thus the knowledge from correct nazar remains necessary or self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT part of the author's response to the objections?
A) He chooses the necessary option and restricts discussion to correct nazar.
B) He chooses the theoretical option and denies infinite regress.
C) He rejects both options and argues that the knowledge is neither necessary nor theoretical.
D) He claims that correct nazar is self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Correct nazar produces knowledge that is self-evident or self-validating, regardless of the errors of incorrect reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in nazar to understand a matter of faith, do you ever pause to ensure your reasoning is correct (النظر الصحيح), or do you assume any reasoning leads to truth? How many times have you been corrected in your understanding? Are you open to that correction?
?
The author argues that correct nazar yields self-evident knowledge. Have you ever experienced a moment of certainty after correct reasoning that felt undeniable? Or do you remain skeptical of your own conclusions? Which stance is more faithful to the method of the Salaf?
?
Consider the objection that many people err after nazar. Do you use the errors of others as an excuse to avoid deep reflection on your own beliefs? What would it take for you to pursue correct nazar without fear of error?

6 min
Answering the Objection of Infinite Regress in Knowledge of No Counterargument
The author refutes the objection that nazar requires knowledge of no counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that nazar requires knowledge of no counterargument, avoiding infinite regress by distinguishing necessary versus theoretical knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge of no counterargument is either self-evident or dispelled by correct reasoning itself.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: if nazar (reasoning) yields knowledge only when we know that no counterargument exists, then that knowledge itself would require another nazar, leading to infinite regress. The author shows that for certainties (qat'iyyat), knowledge of no counterargument is either self-evident (badihi) or implicitly provided by the correctness of the reasoning itself, thus breaking the regress. He also critiques a formulation from al-Tawali' that mischaracterizes the nature of knowledge attained through nazar.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the initial statement of the objection: knowledge of no counterargument must be either necessary or theoretical; if theoretical, it requires another nazar, leading to regress.
Notice the logical structure of the objection.
2
Identify the author's two-part response
The author denies that knowledge of no counterargument is always theoretical. First, for certainties it can be self-evident (necessary). Second, even if it appears theoretical, correct nazar on certainties itself eliminates genuine counterarguments, so no separate proof is needed.
The author uses the phrase 'wa lā nusallimu' to reject key premises.
3
Examine the critique of al-Tawali'
The author criticizes al-Tawali' for claiming that knowledge after nazar is either necessary or theoretical, and both are impossible. He explains that it can be necessary in the sense of compelled assent from premises, which does not contradict being theoretical in origin.
This refines the understanding of necessary vs. theoretical knowledge.
4
Reflect on the solution to infinite regress
Consider how the author's response blocks the regress: by showing that the knowledge of no counterargument is either already present (necessary) or implicitly guaranteed by sound reasoning, no infinite chain of proofs is required.
This preserves the efficacy of nazar for certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما أفاد العلم بالنتيجة أفاد العلم بأن ذلك علم لا جهل أو ظن، وكذا حال العلم بعدم المعارض إذ لا يتصور المعارض للنظر الصحيح في القطعيات، وبهذا تندفع شبهة أخرى وهي أن النظر لو أفاد العلم فلا بد أن يكون مع العلم بعدم المعارض إذ لا جزم مع المعارض . ثم إنه ليس بضروري، إذ كثيراً ما يظهر المعارض بل نظري فيفتقر إلى نظر آخر موقوف على عدم المعارض ويتسلسل فقوله : كالعلم بأنه لا معارض معناه أنه يجوز أن يكون ضرورياً، ولا نسلم ظهور المعارض بعد المنظر الصحيح ، وأن يكون نظرياً ، ولا نسلم توقفه على نظر آخر. وههنا بحث نطلعك عليه في آخر المقصد . وفي تقرير الطوالع ههنا قصور. حيث قال - العلم الحاصل عقيب النظر إما أن يكون ضرورياً أو نظرياً، وكلاهما محال كأنه على حذف المضاف أي علمية العلم الحاصل أعني كونه علماً صح منه اختبار أنه ضروري، وإلا فالحاصل بالنظر لا يكون ضرورياً إلا بمعنى أنا نضطر إلى الجزم به للجزم بالمقدمات، لكنه بهذا المعنى لا يقابل النظرى لهذا
Just as knowledge of the result gives knowledge that it is knowledge and not ignorance or conjecture, so too is the case of knowledge of the absence of a counterargument, because a counterargument is inconceivable for correct reasoning in certainties. And by this, another objection is repelled: that if reasoning yields knowledge, then it must be accompanied by knowledge of the absence of a counterargument, since there is no certainty with a counterargument. Then it (knowledge of the absence) is not necessary (badihi), for a counterargument often appears; rather it is theoretical and thus requires another reasoning dependent on absence of a counterargument, leading to infinite regress. His statement: 'like the knowledge that there is no counterargument' means that it is permissible that it be necessary, and we do not accept the appearance of a counterargument after correct reasoning, and that it be theoretical, and we do not accept its dependence on another reasoning. Here there is a research we will inform you of at the end of the discussion. And in the formulation of al-Tawali' there is deficiency here. He said: 'The knowledge that arises after reasoning is either necessary or theoretical, and both are impossible' — as if with ellipse (of a word), meaning the knowledge-ness of the attained knowledge, i.e., its being knowledge; it is valid from it to test that it is necessary. Otherwise, the knowledge attained through reasoning is not necessary except in the sense that we are compelled to affirm it due to affirming the premises, but in this sense it does not contrast with the theoretical.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The author first establishes that knowledge of the result entails knowing that it is knowledge, not ignorance or conjecture. Similarly, knowledge that there is no counterargument is needed. Then an objection is raised: if reasoning yields knowledge, it must be accompanied by knowledge of no counterargument, but that knowledge itself is either necessary (badihi) or theoretical. If necessary, it is acceptable; if theoretical, it would require another reasoning, leading to infinite regress. The author answers by denying the necessity of such knowledge: the knowledge of no counterargument for certainties is obvious, and if it appears theoretical, it does not require a separate reasoning because correct reasoning itself dispels counterarguments. He then critiques al-Tawali' for claiming that knowledge after reasoning is either necessary or theoretical and both impossible, explaining that it can be necessary in the sense of compelled assent from premises, which does not contradict its being theoretical in origin.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is unavoidable because knowledge of no counterargument would always require another reasoning to verify it.
Correction: The author shows that for certainties, knowledge of no counterargument is either self-evident or implicitly guaranteed by correct reasoning, thus no separate verification is needed and regress is blocked.

Checkpoint
How does the author avoid infinite regress in the requirement for knowledge of no counterargument?
A) He admits that infinite regress is unavoidable.
B) He argues that knowledge of no counterargument is either necessary or, if theoretical, does not require another reasoning because correct reasoning itself eliminates counterarguments.
C) He claims that knowledge of no counterargument is always necessary.
D) He denies that nazar produces knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The knowledge of no counterargument is either self-evident or implicitly provided by correct reasoning, thus no infinite regress arises.

What would it take for you to confidently say, 'No counterargument can stand against the truth'?
?
Think of a time when uncertainty paralyzed you in a matter of belief. Did you actively seek to reason correctly, or did you let the mere possibility of a counterargument shake your conviction? How can you apply this lesson's insight—that correct reasoning itself dispels counterarguments—to rebuild your certainty?
?

Answering the Second Objection: Reasoning's Condition and Its Result
The author refutes the objection that nazar cannot be both productive of knowledge and conditional on its absence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the apparent contradiction between nazar being conditional on ignorance and its being productive of knowledge.

Core Idea
Reasoning's condition (not knowing) applies to its start; knowledge follows at its end.

The objector argues that if reasoning requires not knowing the sought conclusion, then it cannot simultaneously be a cause of that knowledge. The author clarifies that 'entailment' here means subsequence, not simultaneous necessity, so the condition and the result refer to different temporal stages of reasoning. This distinction resolves the paradox.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection points out a contradiction between the condition of nazar (not knowing) and its alleged effect (knowledge).
The objector uses the logical principle that a condition cannot contradict its own result.
2
Understand the key term 'istilzam' and 'isti'qab'
The author redefines 'entalment' (istilzam) as subsequence (isti'qab): knowledge follows nazar after its completion. This is different from simultaneous entailment.
This redefinition is crucial for the answer.
3
Trace the resolution
The condition of not knowing applies to the beginning of nazar, while the knowledge is its endpoint. Thus, no contradiction: the condition and the effect refer to different stages.
Think of a journey: the condition of not having arrived applies to the start, but arriving is the end.
4
Reflect on the importance of temporal stages
Consider how distinguishing different temporal stages in a process can resolve logical paradoxes. This is a key methodological point in kalam.
This is not just about nazar but about causation in general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن النظر مشروط بعدم العلم بالمطلوب، لئلا يلزم طلب الحاصل فلو كان مفيداً للعلم أي مستلزماً له عقلاً أو عادة لما كان مشروطاً بعدمه ضرورة امتناع كون الملزوم مشروطاً بعدم اللازم ورد بأن معنى الاستلزام ههنا الاستعقاب عقلاً أو عادة، بمعنى أنه يلزم حصول العلم بالمطلوب عند تمام النظر، فالملزوم للعلم انتهاؤه، والمشروط بعدم العلم بقاؤه
Second: That reasoning is conditional on not knowing the sought knowledge, to avoid seeking what is already attained. For if it were productive of knowledge, i.e., entailing it intellectually or habitually, it would not be conditional on its absence, necessarily, because it is impossible for the entailing thing to be conditional on the absence of the entailment. It is replied that the meaning of entailment here is subsequence intellectually or habitually, meaning that knowledge of the sought occurs upon completion of reasoning, so the thing entailing knowledge is its endpoint, and what is conditional on absence of knowledge is its continuation.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that nazar (reasoning) is conditional on not already knowing the sought conclusion (otherwise seeking it would be redundant). But if nazar necessarily produces knowledge, then its condition contradicts its effect: the condition (absence of knowledge) would be the opposite of what it causes. The author answers by clarifying that 'entailment' here means subsequence (isti'qab): knowledge follows nazar after its completion. The condition (not knowing) applies to the starting point of nazar, not to its result. Thus, nazar's being conditional on not knowing at its inception does not conflict with its resulting in knowledge at its end.

Key Terms
نظر
علم
مشروط
استلزام
استعقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If nazar is a cause of knowledge, then its condition of not knowing would make it impossible to cause knowledge.
Correction: The condition applies to the state before nazar, not to the state after. Knowledge follows upon completion of nazar.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, what does 'entailment' (istilzam) mean in the context of nazar?
A) Logical necessity at every moment.
B) Subsequence (isti'qab) such that knowledge occurs upon completion of nazar.
C) Simultaneous causation.
D) The condition that nazar must be absent for knowledge to exist.

Key Takeaway
The condition of reasoning (not knowing) applies only to its beginning, not its completion, so there is no contradiction with reasoning resulting in knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you begin a serious search for truth, do you genuinely admit to yourself what you do not know, or do you cling to assumptions that block the path?
?

?
How often do you confuse your initial ignorance (which is necessary for learning) with a permanent state, and let impatience or pride stop you from following the reasoning to its completion?

Answering the Objection on Nazar and Obligation
The author refutes the objection that if nazar yields necessary knowledge, then obligation would be morally bad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that nazar producing necessary knowledge would make obligation morally bad is refuted by distinguishing between the obligation to perform actions (like reasoning) and the resulting knowledge as a quality.

Core Idea
The command is to reason, not to know; knowledge necessarily follows the voluntary act of reasoning.

The objection claims that if nazar necessarily produces knowledge, then that knowledge is involuntary, making it impossible to command and thus morally bad. The author responds by accepting the principle of rational moral evaluation but clarifying that obligation pertains only to voluntary actions, not to the states that result from them. Knowledge is a quality that follows from the voluntary act of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: if nazar yields necessary knowledge, then that knowledge would be involuntary like necessary knowledge, so obligation (taklif) would be morally bad.
Note the assumption that moral badness applies to commanding the involuntary.
2
See the distinction between actions and qualities
The author accepts the principle of rational moral evaluation but distinguishes that obligation applies to actions, not to qualities. Knowledge is a quality, so the command is to perform the actions leading to it (like nazar).
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Theoretical knowledge is voluntary in its acquisition because the obligation is on the voluntary act of reasoning. Its necessary occurrence after the act does not make it involuntary. The possibility of believing the opposite in theoretical propositions confirms this.
Contrast with self-evident propositions where belief is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو أفاد النظر العلم بمعنى لزومه عقيبه عقلاً أو عادة لقبح التكليف بالعلم، لكونه بمنزلة الضرورى في الخروج عن القدرة والاختيار وعن استحقاق الثواب والعقاب، وأجيب بعد تسليم قاعدة القبح العقلي بأن التكليف إنما يكون بالأفعال دون الكيفيات والإضافات والانفعالات والعلم عند المحققين من الكيفيات دون الأفعال، فالتكليف لا يكون إلا بتحصيله، وذلك بمباشرة الأسباب كصرف القوة والنظر واستعمال الحواس، فكان هذا مراد الآمدي بما قال : إن التكليف لم يقع بالمنظور فيه ليصح بل بالنظر وهو مقدور، وإلا فلا خفاء في وقوع التكليف بمعرفة الصانع ووحدانيته ونحو ذلك، وبالجملة، فالعلم النظري مقدور التحصيل والترك، بخلاف الضروري ولزومه بعد تمام النظر لا ينافي ذلك. ومن ههنا أمكن في القضية النظرية اعتقاد النقيض بخلاف القضية البديهية
Third: If reasoning were productive of knowledge in the sense of its being necessary after it intellectually or habitually, then the obligation (taklif) of knowledge would be morally bad, because it would be like necessary knowledge in escaping capability and choice and the deserving of reward and punishment. It is answered, after accepting the principle of rational moral goodness/evil, that obligation only concerns actions, not qualities, relations, and states. And knowledge, according to the verifiers, is among qualities, not actions. So obligation is only to acquire it, and that is by undertaking the causes, such as directing one's power, reasoning, and using the senses. So this was the meaning of al-Amidi when he said: 'Obligation does not pertain to the object of reasoning so that it would be bad, but rather to reasoning, which is within capability.' Otherwise, there is no concealment of the fact that obligation does occur for knowledge of the Maker and His Oneness and the like. In summary, theoretical knowledge is within capability to acquire or leave, unlike necessary knowledge, and its being necessary after the completion of reasoning does not contradict that. And from here, it is possible in a theoretical proposition to believe its opposite, unlike a self-evident proposition.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector says if nazar necessarily produces knowledge, then we cannot be obligated to acquire that knowledge because it would be involuntary like necessary knowledge; thus, obligation (taklif) for such knowledge would be morally bad. The author accepts the principle of rational moral evaluation but distinguishes: obligation pertains to actions, not to qualities or states. Knowledge is a quality, not an action. We are obligated to perform the voluntary actions that lead to knowledge (e.g., reasoning, using senses). Al-Amidi is quoted to clarify that obligation is on nazar (the act of reasoning), not on the object of reasoning. Therefore, theoretical knowledge is voluntary in its acquisition, even if it necessarily follows from voluntary causes. The possibility of believing the opposite in theoretical propositions (unlike self-evident ones) confirms its voluntary nature.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If nazar necessarily produces knowledge, then knowledge is involuntary and cannot be commanded.
Correction: Knowledge is a quality; the command is on the voluntary act of reasoning that produces it. The knowledge itself necessarily follows but the act is voluntary.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that theoretical knowledge can be subject to obligation despite necessarily following nazar?
A) Because the obligation is on the knowledge itself, which is voluntary.
B) Because the obligation is on the action of reasoning, which is voluntary, and knowledge necessarily follows it.
C) Because nazar does not actually produce knowledge.
D) Because knowledge is an action.

Key Takeaway
Theoretical knowledge is commanded because the command is on the voluntary act of reasoning, not on the resulting quality of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Answering the Objection from Self-Knowledge
The author refutes the objection that the difficulty in knowing the self proves the impossibility of knowing the divine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between difficulty and impossibility in rational inquiry, and how the author refutes an objection based on the uncertainty of self-knowledge.

Core Idea
Difficulty is not impossibility; reason can still attain certainty.

The objector argues that if we cannot even know our own self with certainty, how can we know Allah? In this passage, the author shows that such uncertainty only reflects difficulty, not impossibility. Let's examine the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully and identify the objector's argument: even the self (huwiyya) is uncertain, so more distant matters are even less knowable.
Notice the list of possible identities for the self: sensible structure, subtle particles, indivisible part, etc.
2
Understand the answer
The author responds by distinguishing difficulty from impossibility. He says the uncertainty only indicates difficulty (ṣu‘ūba), not impossibility (imtinā‘).
The point of contention is whether reasoning can yield knowledge at all, not whether it is easy.
3
Apply the lesson
Explain why this distinction is crucial: if difficulty were proof of impossibility, then all difficult sciences would be impossible, which is false.
This refutes the objection without conceding the impossibility of divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن أقرب الأشياء إلى الإنسان اتصالاً ومناسبة هويته التي يشير إليها بقوله : أنا . وقد كثر فيها الخلاف، ولم يحصل من النظر الجزم بأنها هذا الهيكل المحسوس أو أجزاء لطيفة ساريه فيه، أو جزء لا يتجزأ في القلب، أو جوهر مجرد متعلق به أو غير ذلك فكيف فيما هو أبعد كالسماوات والعناصر وعجائب المركبات وأبعد كالمجردات والإلهيات ومباحث الذات والصفات ؟ وأجيب : بأن ذلك إنما يدل على صعوبة تحصيل هذه العلوم بالنظر لا على امتناعه والمتنازع هو الامتناع لا الصعوبة
Fourth: The closest of things to a human in connection and suitability is his identity (huwiyya) which he points to by saying 'I'. Yet controversy has abounded concerning it, and reasoning has not yielded certainty whether it is this sensible structure or subtle particles permeating it, or an indivisible part in the heart, or a simple substance attached to it, or something else. So how about what is more distant, like the heavens, the elements, wonders of compounds, and even more distant like immaterial entities, divine matters, and investigations of essence and attributes? And it is answered: That only indicates the difficulty of acquiring these sciences through reasoning, not its impossibility; and the point of contention is impossibility, not difficulty.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that even the self (huwiyya), which is the closest thing to a person, is subject to disagreement and uncertainty through reasoning; multiple contradictory views exist. If reasoning cannot yield certainty about the self, then it is even less likely to yield certainty about more distant matters (heavens, elements, immaterial entities, divine attributes). The author responds by distinguishing difficulty from impossibility: the lack of certainty about the self only shows that attaining that knowledge is difficult, not that it is absolutely impossible. The issue at hand is whether reasoning can produce knowledge at all, not whether it is easy.

Misconception: That uncertainty about the self proves reasoning cannot attain knowledge of the divine.
Correction: The author clarifies that uncertainty only shows difficulty, not logical impossibility.

Checkpoint

A) He agrees it is impossible.
B) He says the self is not knowable by reason.
C) He says the uncertainty only indicates difficulty, not impossibility.
D) He says divine matters are easier than the self.

Key Takeaway
The difficulty in knowing the self through reason does not prove that divine knowledge is impossible; it only shows that such knowledge is challenging.

Answering the Fifth Objection: Conceptualization and Reasoning
Objection-and-answer regarding conceptualization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a partial description (rasm) suffices for conceptualization in divine reasoning and how the author refutes the objection that perfect definition is necessary.

Core Idea
You don't need perfect definition to reason about God; a description is enough.

The author of Sharh al-Maqasid now addresses a subtle objection: if reasoning (nazar) requires conceptualization (tasawwur), and conceptualization of divine matters is impossible, then reasoning is futile. The objector argues that definition (hadd) is impossible for incomposite beings and description (rasm) fails to capture true quiddity. In response, the author shows that description can indeed convey the essence, and even a minimal conceptualization suffices for reasoning to proceed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal English translation. Focus on the overall argument structure.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection and its premises
The objector claims reasoning (nazar) is impossible because its condition—conceptualization—cannot be achieved. Find the two ways conceptualization could fail (innate or acquired) and why acquired fails.
Note the terms: hadd (definition) and rasm (description).
3
Understand the author's answer
The author replies that description (rasm) can yield conceptualization of the quiddity even if not fully. Also, if that is insufficient, a minimal conceptualization (tasawwur bi-wajh ma) is enough.
The answer does not require full knowledge of essence.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is this answer important for the possibility of theological reasoning? How does it protect the discipline of kalam from skeptical objections?
This connects to the broader methodology of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : لو أفاد النظر العلم أي التصديق في الإلهيات لكان شرطه هو التصور متحققاً لكنه منتف إما بالضرورة فظاهر وإما بالكسب فلأن الحد ممتنع لامتناع التركيب والرسم لا يفيد تصور الحقيقة. وأجيب بأن الرسم قد يفيد تصور الحقيقة وإن لم يستلزمه ، ولو سلم فيكفي التصور بوجه ما
Fifth: If reasoning produced knowledge, i.e., assent (tasdiq), in divine matters, then its condition would be conceptualization (tasawwur) realized, but it is absent: either by necessity, which is obvious, or by acquisition, because definition (hadd) is impossible due to impossibility of composition, and description (rasm) does not yield conceptualization of the quiddity. And it is answered that description (rasm) may yield conceptualization of the quiddity even if it does not necessitate it; and if it is granted, then a conceptualization in some manner suffices.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that reasoning (nazar) aims for assent (tasdiq) in divine matters, but prior conceptualization (tasawwur) of the terms is necessary. However, such conceptualization is impossible: if it is innate (daruri), that is not the case; if acquired, it requires definition (hadd), which is impossible because divine entities are incomposite, and description (rasm) does not convey true quiddity. The author answers that rasm (description) can indeed yield conceptualization of the quiddity, even if it does not entail full comprehension. Moreover, even if rasm is insufficient, a general conceptualization (tasawwur bi-wajh ma) suffices for reasoning to proceed.

Key Terms
تصديق
تصور
حد
رسم
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that full definitional knowledge of divine essences is necessary for reasoning.
Correction: The author shows that a partial description (rasm) is sufficient to provide the needed conceptualization.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the fifth objection, what serves as an acceptable conceptualization (tasawwur) for reasoning in divine matters?
A) A complete definition (hadd).
B) A description (rasm) that provides a partial understanding.
C) Only innate knowledge.
D) No conceptualization is needed.

Key Takeaway
Description (rasm) is sufficient to provide the conceptualization needed for reasoning about divine matters, even if it does not yield full quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson says even a partial description of God suffices for reasoning. Are you content with a partial description of God in your worship, or do you secretly yearn for a full definition that no one can have? How does that yearning hinder your trust in what has been revealed?
?
The objection claims that without perfect conceptualization, reasoning about God is impossible. Have you ever used your lack of complete knowledge as an excuse to avoid deep reflection on God? How does this lesson challenge that excuse, and what step will you take this week to think more about God?
?
The answer relies on 'a conceptualization in some manner.' Do you have any conceptualization of God at all? If not, what prevents you from seeking that minimal understanding? If yes, how do you actively build upon it in your daily life to grow closer to Him?

5 min
Does Faulty Reasoning Necessarily Lead to Ignorance?
Al-Imam al-Ghazali's view and the material/formal distinction introduced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial disagreement about whether faulty reasoning leads to ignorance, and the two main positions: al-Imam al-Ghazali's view that it does, and the view that it depends on the type of fault.

Core Idea
Faulty reasoning does not always lead to ignorance – the type of fault matters.

This lesson introduces a key debate in kalam methodology: does flawed reasoning inevitably produce false belief? Scholars who affirm that correct reasoning yields knowledge disagree on this subsidiary issue. Al-Imam al-Ghazali argues that faulty reasoning does necessitate ignorance, while others distinguish between formal and material flaws.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the two initial positions.
Notice how al-Imam's example is structured.
2
Identify the two views
Identify al-Imam's view and the alternative view. Notice the conditional phrasing 'if the fault is confined to the matter'.
3
Reflect on the example
Examine al-Imam's example: believing the world is eternal and every eternal is independent. Why does he claim this necessarily leads to ignorance?
The conclusion follows necessarily from the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأن النظر الصحيح المقرون بشرائطه يستلزم العلم، اختلفوا في أن النظر الفاسد (۱) قد يستلزم الجهل، أي الاعتقاد الغير المطابق. فقال الإمام: يستلزمه لأن من اعتقد أن العالم قديم، وكل قديم مستغن عن المؤثر استحال أن لا يعتقد أن العالم غني عن المؤثر. وقيل: إن كان الفساد مقصوراً على المادة يستلزمه وإلا فلا. أما الأول فلأن لزوم النتيجة للقياس المشتمل على الشرائط ضروري ابتداء أو انتهاء، سواء كانت المقدمات صادقة أو كاذبة، كما في المثال المذكور. وأما الثاني: فلأن معنى فساد الصورة أنه ليس من الضروب التي تلزمها النتيجة، والصحيح أنه لا يستلزم الجهل على التقديرين، أما عند فساد الصورة فظاهر كما مر (۲) وأما عند فساد المادة فقط بأن تكون الصورة من الضروب المنتجة (۳) فلأن اللازم من الكاذب قد لا يكون كاذباً كما إذا اعتقد أن العالم أثر الموجب بالذات وكل ما هو أثر الموجب بالذات فهو حادث، فإنه يستلزم أن العالم حادث. وهو حق مع كذب القياس بمقدمتيه نعم قد يفيد الجهل كما إذا اعتقد أن العالم قديم (٤) وكل قديم مستغن عن المؤثر. والتحقيق: أنه لا نزاع في أن الفاسد صورة لا يستلزم بالاتفاق، والفاسد مادة فقط قد يستلزم وقد لا يستلزم فمراد الإمام الإيجاب الجزئي كما في المثال المذكور. ومرادنا نفي الإيجاب الكلي لعدم اللزوم في بعض المواد، والقائلون بأنه لا لزوم أصلاً يريدون اللزوم الذي مناطه صفة في الشبهة، بمعنى أن شبهة المنظور فيها ليس لها (۱) لذاتها صفة ولا وجه بكونه مناط للملازمة بينها وبين المطلوب وإلا لما انتفت الدلالة (۲) والعناصر، وعجائب المركبات وأبعد كالمجردات والإلهيات من مباحث الذات والصفات وأجيب بأن ذلك إنما يدل على صعوبة تحصيل هذه العلوم بالنظر لا على امتناعه والمتنازع هو الامتناع لا الصعوبة.
Those who say that correct reasoning combined with its conditions necessitates knowledge disagreed about whether faulty reasoning (1) might necessitate ignorance, i.e., belief that is not corresponding. Al-Imam said: It necessitates it because whoever believes that the world is eternal, and every eternal is independent of an effect, it is impossible that he not believe that the world is independent of an effect. And it was said: If the fault is confined to the matter, it necessitates it; otherwise not. As for the first, because the implication of the conclusion for a syllogism containing the conditions is necessary from the beginning or the end, whether the premises are true or false, as in the mentioned example. As for the second, because the meaning of formal fault is that it is not among the productive forms, and the correct view is that it does not necessitate ignorance on both accounts; as for formal fault, it is clear as earlier (2); and as for material fault only, when the form is among the productive forms (3), because the necessary from a false premise may not be false, as when one believes that the world is the effect of the essentially necessitating cause and everything that is the effect of the essentially necessitating cause is originated, then it necessitates that the world is originated—and this is true despite the falsity of the syllogism in both premises—indeed it may produce ignorance, as when one believes that the world is eternal (4) and every eternal is independent of an effect. The verification: there is no dispute that what is formally faulty never necessitates by agreement, and what is materially faulty only may necessitate and may not. So al-Imam meant partial affirmation as in the mentioned example. And our meaning is the negation of universal affirmation because of lack of implication in some matters, and those who say there is no implication at all mean the implication whose basis is a property in the specious argument, meaning that the specious argument in the object of reasoning has no property in itself nor aspect by which it is a basis for the implication between it and the sought, else indication (2) and the elements, wonders of compounds, and even more like abstractions and divine matters from the investigations of essence and attributes would not be absent; and it was answered that this only indicates the difficulty of acquiring these sciences through reasoning, not its impossibility, and the point of dispute is impossibility, not difficulty.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical analysis of whether faulty reasoning (النظر الفاسد) necessarily leads to ignorance (الجهل). It begins by noting that those who affirm correct reasoning yields knowledge disagree on this subsidiary issue. Al-Imam (al-Ghazali) holds that faulty reasoning does necessitate ignorance, giving the example of inferring from 'the world is eternal and every eternal is independent of an effect' to 'the world is independent of an effect'—a conclusion that is false and therefore ignorance. A second view distinguishes between formal fault (فساد الصورة) and material fault (فساد المادة): if the fault is only in the matter, it may necessitate ignorance; if in the form, it does not. The author then explains each position: for the first, a formally valid syllogism necessarily yields a conclusion, regardless of truth of premises; for the second, formal fault means the form is not productive, so no conclusion follows. The correct view (الصحيح) is that formal fault never necessitates ignorance, while material fault sometimes does and sometimes does not—a false premise may still yield a true conclusion, as in the example of believing the world is the effect of the essentially necessitating cause (الموجب بالذات) and that every such effect is originated, which correctly concludes the world is originated, even though the syllogism's premises are false. Conversely, material fault can produce ignorance, as in the eternal-and-independent example. The author then states the verification (التحقيق): there is consensus that formal fault never implies ignorance, and material fault may or may not. Thus al-Imam's view is interpreted as partial affirmation (الإيجاب الجزئي), not universal. The author clarifies that his own intent is to negate universal affirmation (نفي الإيجاب الكلي) because implication fails in some cases. He then addresses those who deny any implication at all from faulty reasoning: they argue that a specious argument (شبهة) has no inherent property to necessitate the conclusion, otherwise signs (دلالات) and elements would never fail. The answer is that this only shows the difficulty (صعوبة) of attaining these sciences through reasoning, not its impossibility (امتناع); the real point of contention is impossibility, not difficulty.

Key Terms
النظر الفاسد
الجهل
فساد المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any faulty reasoning always leads to ignorance.
Correction: The passage shows that some faulty reasoning (formal fault) does not necessitate ignorance, and material fault may or may not.

Checkpoint
What example does al-Imam give to support his claim that faulty reasoning necessitates ignorance?
A) The world is eternal, and every eternal is independent, so the world is independent.
B) The world is created, and every created has a creator, so the world has a creator.
C) The world is the effect of the essentially necessitating cause, and every such effect is originated, so the world is originated.
D) The world is contingent, and every contingent needs a cause, so the world needs a cause.

Key Takeaway
Al-Imam argues that faulty reasoning necessitates ignorance, but others restrict it to material fault.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a belief you hold that contradicts Islamic teachings, do you honestly examine whether the error is in the material (your assumptions) or the form (your reasoning)? Will you today audit one belief against the Quran and Sunnah to see where the fault lies?
?
Al-Imam's example shows that a false premise inevitably leads to a false conclusion. What premise do you hold about yourself, your life, or your Lord that may not be grounded in revelation? Will you bring it before the scholars until you achieve correct knowledge?
?
If you know someone who holds a flawed argument against Islam, do you immediately assume ignorance or ill will? How can al-Imam's distinction between material and formal fault change your approach when you next debate? Will you commit to diagnosing the type of error before you react?

6 min
The Correct View: Formal Fault vs Material Fault
Explanation of formal and material fault with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why formal fault never necessitates ignorance, while material fault sometimes does, and the role of productive syllogistic forms.

Core Idea
A formally faulty argument yields no conclusion; a materially faulty one may still produce truth by accident.

Having learned the two initial positions, we now examine the reasoning behind them. The text explains that a syllogism containing its conditions necessarily implies its conclusion, regardless of truth of premises. But if the form is faulty, no conclusion follows. Material fault, however, can sometimes yield a true conclusion, as in the example of the essentially necessitating cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of the first view
Read the passage explaining the reasoning of the first view (that material fault necessitates ignorance). Note that a valid syllogism inevitably yields a conclusion.
This is the principle of logical implication.
2
Understand formal fault
The passage defines formal fault as not being among the productive forms. When the form is faulty, no conclusion follows, so ignorance cannot be necessitated.
3
Study the examples of material fault
Two contrasting examples are given: one where false premises lead to a true conclusion (the world is originated), and another leading to false conclusion (the world is independent). This shows material fault can go either way.
Notice the key term 'الموجب بالذات' (essentially necessitating cause).

?

?

The Verification: Partial vs Universal Affirmation
Final verification of the dispute and clarification of the nature of implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final resolution: formal fault never implies ignorance, material fault sometimes does; the dispute is about universal vs partial affirmation; and the response to those who deny any implication.

Core Idea
Al-Imam meant partial affirmation, not universal; implication depends on the material.

After analyzing the examples, the text provides the final verification (التحقيق). It clarifies that there is consensus on formal fault, and material fault is contingent. Al-Imam's view is interpreted as partial affirmation, and the author negates universal affirmation. The passage also addresses those who deny any implication from faulty reasoning, arguing their position would lead to the impossibility of signs and wonders.

Guided Path
1
Read the verification statement
Read the passage starting with 'والتحقيق'. Note the consensus on formal fault and the contingent nature of material fault.
This is the author's own resolution.
2
Understand partial vs universal
The text distinguishes between partial affirmation (الإيجاب الجزئي) that al-Imam meant, and universal affirmation (الإيجاب الكلي) which the author negates. Material fault sometimes implies ignorance.
3
Consider the objection and response
The passage mentions those who deny any implication at all, arguing that a specious argument (شبهة) has no inherent property for implication. The response is that this only shows difficulty, not impossibility.
The key distinction is between difficulty (صعوبة) and impossibility (امتناع).

This passage is a dialectical analysis of whether faulty reasoning (النظر الفاسد) necessarily leads to ignorance (الجهل). It begins by noting that those who affirm correct reasoning yields knowledge disagree on this subsidiary issue. Al-Imam (al-Ghazali) holds that faulty reasoning does necessitate ignorance, giving the example of inferring from 'the world is eternal and every eternal is independent of an effect' to 'the world is independent of an effect'—a conclusion that is false and therefore ignorance. A second view distinguishes between formal fault (فساد الصورة) and material fault (فساد المادة): if the fault is only in the matter, it may necessitate ignorance; if in the form, it does not. The author then explains each position: for the first, a formally valid syllogism necessarily yields a conclusion, regardless of truth of premises; for the second, formal fault means the form is not productive, so no conclusion follows. The correct view (الصحيح) is that formal fault never necessitates ignorance, while material fault sometimes does and sometimes does not—a false premise may still yield a true conclusion, as in the example of believing the world is the effect of the essentially necessitating cause (الموجب بالذات) and that every such effect is originated, which correctly concludes the world is originated, even though the syllogism's premises are false. Conversely, material fault can produce ignorance, as in the eternal-and-independent example. The author then states the verification (التحقيق): there is consensus that formal fault never implies ignorance, and material fault may or may not. Thus al-Imam's view is interpreted as partial affirmation (الإيجاب الجزئي), not universal. The author clarifies that his own intent is to negate universal affirmation (نفي الإيجاب الكلي) because implication fails in some cases. He then addresses those who deny any implication at all from faulty reasoning: they argue that a specious argument (شبهة) has no inherent property to necessitate the conclusion, otherwise signs (دلالات) and elements would never fail. The answer is that this only shows the difficulty (صعوبة) of attaining these sciences through reasoning, not its impossibility (امتناع); the real point of contention is impossibility, not difficulty.

Key Terms
الإيجاب الجزئي
الإيجاب الكلي
الشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is no implication at all from faulty reasoning.
Correction: The text affirms that material fault can imply ignorance in some cases; complete denial is refuted.

Checkpoint
According to the verification, what is the status of formal fault regarding ignorance?
A) It always necessitates ignorance.
B) It never necessitates ignorance by agreement.
C) It sometimes necessitates ignorance.
D) It necessitates ignorance only if the matter is also faulty.

Key Takeaway
Formal fault never implies ignorance; material fault may or may not; the dispute is about universality, not existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find an argument in the deen difficult to understand, do you treat it as impossible? Can you recall a specific topic you dismissed as beyond reason? What would it take for you to distinguish difficulty from impossibility in that case?
?
The text distinguishes between formal and material fault in reasoning. In your own search for truth, do you examine whether your mistakes lie in how you think or in what you assume? When did you last attribute a misunderstanding to a formal error rather than a material one?
?
This lesson shows that the dispute is about universality, not existence. Are there beliefs you hold universally that might only be partially true? If you found an exception to one of your universal claims, would you adjust your belief or reject the exception? How does your answer reflect on your humility before truth?

Is Conception of Divine Realities Impossible? The Fifth Objection Answered
Refutes the claim that knowledge of divine matters requires unattainable conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine realities cannot be conceived and how the answer of kalam scholars defends the possibility of rational knowledge of Allah.

Core Idea
A full definition is not needed for valid conception; a descriptive sketch suffices for reasoning about Allah.

In this lesson, we encounter a key objection raised against the very project of kalam: that we cannot even form a concept of divine realities, so how can we reason about them? The objection hinges on the impossibility of defining Allah. The response, however, shows that a complete definition is not a necessary condition for acquiring knowledge through reasoning. Let's examine the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal English translation. Let the flow of the objection and answer come through.
Notice the structure: 'لو أفاد...' states the conditional, then 'لكنه منتف' gives the objection, then 'وأجيب' introduces the answer.
2
Identify the two parts of the objection
The objection claims conception is unattainable. It is either necessarily impossible (obvious) or impossible by acquisition. Focus on the acquired path: hadd is impossible due to lack of composition, and rasm does not give true forms.
Hadd is a definition by genus and differentia; rasm is a description by properties.
3
Examine the response carefully
The answer makes two points: (1) rasm can give conception of reality even if it doesn't necessitate it, and (2) even if that is not granted, a minimal conception (li-wajh ma) suffices. Understand how this counters the objection.
The phrase 'ولو سلم فيكفي التصور لوجه ما' is crucial: it shows that a partial conception is enough to start reasoning.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is this defense important for kalam? If the objection stood, rational theology would be impossible. The answer preserves the possibility of acquiring assent about Allah through reasoning, even if we cannot fully define His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: لو أفاد النظر العلم أي التصديق في الحقائق الإلهيات لكان شرطه وهو التصور متحققاً لكنه منتف إما بالضرورة فظاهر، وإما بالكسب فلأن الحد ممتنع لامتناع التركيب وللرسم لا يفيد تصور الصور الحقيقية. وأجيب بأن الرسم (۳) قد يفيد تصور الحقيقة وإن لم يستلزمه ولو سلم فيكفي التصور لوجه ما.
Fifth: If reasoning yielded knowledge, i.e., assent, concerning divine realities, then its condition, which is conception, would be realized, but it is absent—either necessarily, which is obvious, or by acquisition, because definition (حد) is impossible due to the impossibility of composition, and description (رسم) does not yield conception of true forms. And it was answered that description (3) may yield conception of reality even if it does not necessitate it, and if granted, conception for some aspect suffices.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection (الخامس) against the claim that reasoning can yield knowledge (التصديق) in divine matters. The objection argues that knowledge requires conception (التصور) as a condition, but such conception is unattainable. It is unattainable either necessarily (which is obvious) or by acquisition: definition (الحد) is impossible because divine realities are simple and cannot be composed of genus and differentia, and description (الرسم) does not give true conception of the real forms. The answer (أجيب) is that description (الرسم) can indeed give conception of the reality even if it does not necessitate full comprehension, and even if this is not granted, a minimal conception for some aspect (لوجه ما) suffices for reasoning to proceed.

Key Terms
التصور
التصديق
الحد
الرسم
الحقائق الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any knowledge of Allah requires full comprehension of His essence; this passage clarifies that a partial conception is enough for rational inquiry.
Correction: A full definition is not necessary; a descriptive conception or even a minimal conceptual grasp suffices to begin reasoning and acquire assent.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the objection that definition (حد) is impossible for divine realities?
A) It is admitted, so reasoning is impossible.
B) Description (رسم) can still provide conception of the reality.
C) Definition is possible through analogy.
D) Conception is not needed for assent.

Key Takeaway
Even without a full definition, a descriptive conception suffices to begin reasoning about Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objector claimed divine matters are unknowable, but the answer shows reasoning is possible. What truth about Allah have you been neglecting because it seems too complex, and how will you use the description you have to draw closer to Him?
?
If only a descriptive sketch suffices for valid conception, then you already have enough to start reasoning about Allah. What is one attribute of Allah you can think about right now using the basic understanding you possess, and how will that reflection change your actions today?

Valid Reflection and the Four Key Terms
Al-Taftazani defines valid reflection conditions and four logical terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for valid reflection in Islamic theology and the distinct meanings of the four key terms: proof, proven, indication, and aspect of indication.

Core Idea
Valid reflection requires focusing on a real proof from its proper indicating aspect.

Al-Taftazani begins by establishing the general conditions for any reflection, then specifies what makes reflection valid. He introduces four essential terms that theologians use to analyze proofs. This passage is the foundation for understanding how arguments work in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's terminology sink in.
Notice the technical terms: نظر, دليل, مدلول, دلالة, جهة.
2
Identify the two conditions for valid reflection
The passage states two conditions: (1) reflection must be on a real proof (dalil) not a sophism (shubha), and (2) it must be from the aspect of its indication (jihah dalalah) not another aspect.
This distinguishes valid from invalid theological reasoning.
3
Distinguish the four terms using the example
Use the world-Creator example to see each term: world (dalil), existence of Creator (madlul), the fact that reflection gives knowledge (dalalah), and contingency/origination (jihah dalalah). Notice they are distinct but connected.
The aspect of indication is the specific property that links proof to proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثالث : يشترط لمطلق (۱) النظر صحيحاً كان أو فاسداً(۲) بعد شرائط العلم - عدم الجزم بالمطلوب أو بنقيضه إذ لا طلب مع الأدلة إنما هو لزيادة الاطمئنان أو التحصيل استعداد القبول في المتعلم بالإجماع أو في كل متعلم بدليل آخر . وقال الإمام المطلوب بالدليل الثاني في كونه دليلاً وهو غير معلوم، ويشترط للنظر الصحيح أن يكون في الدليل دون الشبهة ومن جهة دلالته دون غيرها، وهي الأمر الذي بواسطته ينتقل الذهن من الدليل إلى المدلول كإمكان (۳) العالم أو حدوثه لثبوت الصانع، فالعالم هو الدليل وثبوت الصانع هو المدلول، وكونه بحيث يفيد النظر فيه العلم بثبوت الصانع هو الدلالة، وإمكانه وحدوثه هو جهة الدلالة، وهذه الأمور متغايرة فتتغاير العلوم المتعلقة بها إلا أن جهة الدلالة شديدة الاتصال بالمدلول. فمن ههنا توهم أن العلم بها نفس العلم بالمدلول).
He said: The third section: It is required for absolute reflection, whether correct or incorrect, after the conditions of knowledge: not being certain of the sought or its opposite, for there is no seeking with proofs; it is only for increasing reassurance or acquisition or preparation of acceptance in the learner by consensus or in every learner according to another proof. And the Imam said: The sought by the second proof is that it is a proof and it is unknown. And it is required for valid reflection that it be in the proof and not in the sophism, and from the aspect of its indication and not from other than it. And it (the indication) is the thing by means of which the mind moves from the proof to the proven, such as the possibility or origination of the world for the establishment of the Creator. So the world is the proof, and the establishment of the Creator is the proven. And its being such that reflection in it yields knowledge of the establishment of the Creator is the indication. And its possibility and origination are the aspect of indication. These matters are distinct, so the sciences related to them are distinct, except that the aspect of indication is strongly connected to the proven. Hence it was imagined that knowledge of it is the very knowledge of the proven.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for valid reflection (al-nazar al-sahih) and clarifies the four essential terms. Al-Taftazani first reiterates the condition for absolute reflection: not being certain of the sought or its opposite. He then quotes an Imam (likely al-Razi) about the sought being unknown. Then he states the two conditions for valid reflection: first, that the reflection be directed at a proof (dalil) and not at a sophism (shubha); second, that it be from the aspect of its indication (jihah dalalah) and not from another aspect. He defines the indication (al-dalalah) as that by means of which the mind moves from the proof to the proven (al-madlul). He gives the example: the world's contingency or origination (imkan al-ʿalam aw huduthuhu) as the aspect of indication that the world is proof for the existence of the Creator. Thus, the world is the proof (dalil), the existence of the Creator is the proven (madlul), and the fact that reflection on the world yields knowledge of the Creator is the indication (dalalah). The specific property of contingency or origination is the aspect of indication (jihah al-dalalah). These four are distinct, so the sciences related to them are distinct, but the aspect of indication is closely connected to the proven, which led some to mistakenly think that knowledge of the aspect is identical to knowledge of the proven.

Key Terms
النظر الصحيح
الدليل
المدلول
الدلالة
جهة الدلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of the aspect of indication (jihah al-dalalah) is the same as knowledge of the proven (madlul).
Correction: The aspect of indication is the specific property of the proof that leads to the proven, but it is distinct from the proven itself. Knowing the aspect is not the same as knowing the existence of the proven.

Checkpoint
In the example given by al-Taftazani, what is the aspect of indication (jihah al-dalalah) for proving the existence of the Creator?
A) The world itself.
B) The existence of the Creator.
C) The contingency or origination of the world.
D) The act of reflection.

Key Takeaway
The four terms—proof, proven, indication, and aspect of indication—are distinct yet interconnected; valid reflection must be directed at the indication aspect of a real proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the world around you, do you actively seek the aspect of indication that leads to the Creator, or do you content yourself with knowing that the world exists? What would change if you trained yourself to look for that specific aspect?
?
Have you ever assumed that understanding the mechanics of a proof—like the argument from contingency—is the same as actually knowing God? How can you move from intellectual awareness to genuine conviction that changes your life?
?
In your pursuit of knowledge, have you ever been certain of a conclusion before properly examining the proof from its correct aspect? What would it look like to hold your beliefs with humility, always open to valid reflection?

The Two Conditions for Reflection and the Objection Regarding Compound Ignorance
Al-Taftazani enumerates two conditions for absolute reflection and refutes an objection about compound ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani lists absence of compound ignorance as a separate condition for reflection and how he defends this against the charge of redundancy.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is a distinct obstacle to reflection because it is certainty in the opposite, not mere ignorance.

Al-Taftazani continues his discussion of the prerequisites for valid theological reflection. Having already mentioned general conditions like life and intellect, he now specifies two conditions that are directly relevant to the act of seeking knowledge. The second condition—absence of compound ignorance—requires careful explanation, as some may think it is already covered by the conditions of knowledge. This passage clarifies why it must be stated explicitly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Taftazani uses a very precise and technical style.
2
Identify the two conditions
The passage presents two conditions for reflection. Find them and note how al-Taftazani defines each one. The first is absence of knowledge of the sought, the second is absence of compound ignorance of it.
Compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) is certainty in the opposite of the sought.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
Read the objection that claims compound ignorance is already included under the conditions of knowledge. Then see how al-Taftazani refutes it by distinguishing between knowledge in general and knowledge of the sought specifically.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the independence of the two conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للنظر صحيحاً كان أو فاسداً بعد شرائط العلم من الحياة والعقل وعدم النوم والغفلة ونحو ذلك، أمران . أحدهما : عدم العلم بالمطلوب إذ لا طلب مع الحصول . ثانيهما: عدم الجهل المركب به أعني عدم الجزم بنقيضه، لأن ذلك من الإقدام على الطلب إما لأن النظر يجب أن يكون مقارناً للشك على ما هو رأي أبي هاشم . والجهل المركب مقارن للجزم فيتناقضان وإما لأن الجهل المركب صارف عنه كالأكل مع الامتلاء على ما هو رأي الحكماء، ومن أن النظر أن يكون مع الشك. وإليه ذهب القاضي، بل ذهب الأستاذ إلى أن الناظر يمتنع أن يكون شاكاً، وما ذكرنا مع وجازته أوضح مما قال في المواقف: إن شرط النظر مطلقاً بعد الحياة، أمران : الأول : وجود العقل، والثاني : عدم ضده أي ضد النظر فمنه أي من ضده ما هو عام أي ضد للنظر ولكل إدراك ( كالنوم والغفلة مثلاً ومنه ما هو خاص أي ضد للنظر دون الإدراكات وهو العلم بالمطلوب والجهل المركب به. فإن قيل الجهل المركب ضد للعلم فانتفاؤه مندرج في شرائط العلم فيكون في عبارتكم استدراك . قلنا : الجهل المركب بالمطلوب يكون ضداً للعلم به لا للعلم على .
For reflection, whether correct or incorrect, after the conditions of knowledge such as life, intellect, absence of sleep and heedlessness and the like, there are two matters. First: absence of knowledge of the sought, for there is no seeking with attainment. Second: absence of compound ignorance concerning it, meaning absence of certainty in its opposite, because that (certainty) prevents undertaking the search – either because reflection must be accompanied by doubt, according to the opinion of Abu Hashim, and compound ignorance is accompanied by certainty, so they are contradictory; or because compound ignorance repels it (reflection) like eating with fullness, according to the opinion of the philosophers, and from the fact that reflection is with doubt. The judge (al-Qadi) held this view, indeed the master (al-Ustadh) held that the reflector cannot be in doubt. And what we have mentioned, despite its conciseness, is clearer than what was said in al-Mawaqif: 'Verily, the condition for absolute reflection after life is two matters: first, existence of intellect, and second, absence of its opposite, i.e., the opposite of reflection. Among its opposites are general ones, i.e., opposites to reflection and to every perception, such as sleep and heedlessness for example; and among them are specific ones, i.e., opposites to reflection but not to perceptions, which are knowledge of the sought and compound ignorance concerning it.' If it is said: 'Compound ignorance is an opposite of knowledge, so its negation is included under the conditions of knowledge, thus in your expression there is redundancy.' We say: 'Compound ignorance concerning the sought is an opposite to knowledge of it, not to knowledge in general.'
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage enumerates the conditions for absolute reflection (al-nazar mutlaqan) and then addresses an objection regarding compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab). Al-Taftazani begins by stating that for reflection, whether correct or incorrect, after the conditions of knowledge (life, intellect, absence of sleep and heedlessness), there are two conditions. First: absence of knowledge of the sought (al-matlub), because if one already knows it, there is no need for seeking. Second: absence of compound ignorance of it, meaning absence of certainty in its opposite. He then explains that this second condition is necessary because compound ignorance either conflicts with the required state of doubt (as per Abu Hashim) or repels the act of reflection like eating when full (as per the philosophers). He notes that the judge (al-Qadi) and the master (al-Ustadh) had different views on whether the reflector must be in a state of doubt. He then compares his formulation to that of al-Mawaqif, which states that the conditions are existence of intellect and absence of its opposite, where the opposite includes both general opposites (like sleep and heedlessness) and specific ones (knowledge of the sought and compound ignorance). The objection is raised: compound ignorance is an opposite of knowledge, so its negation is already included under the conditions of knowledge, making it redundant. Al-Taftazani answers: compound ignorance of the sought is opposite to knowledge of the sought, not to knowledge in general, so it is specifically relevant to reflection.

Key Terms
النظر
الجهل المركب
شرائط العلم
المطلوب
الجزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Compound ignorance is just a subset of ignorance and does not need a separate condition because its negation is already covered by the conditions of knowledge.
Correction: Compound ignorance (certainty in the opposite) is specifically opposed to knowledge of the sought, not to knowledge in general. Therefore, it requires a distinct condition to ensure the reflector is not certain of the opposite of what is sought.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, why is compound ignorance considered a separate condition for reflection?
A) Because it is a type of knowledge.
B) Because it constitutes certainty in the opposite, which prevents the seeker from seeking.
C) Because it is the same as simple ignorance.
D) Because it is already included under the conditions of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance is certainty in the opposite of what is sought, and it requires a separate condition for reflection because it specifically opposes seeking, unlike general ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been certain of something about your faith without ever truly reflecting on it? What is that one belief where you might have compound ignorance—certainty without investigation—and are you willing to honestly re-examine it today?
?
This lesson teaches that compound ignorance prevents reflection because it repels it like fullness repels eating. Is there a truth you have been avoiding because you feel 'full'—satisfied with your current understanding? What would it cost you to admit ignorance and seek again?
?
If reflection requires doubt, then your certainty about some matters might actually be an obstacle to deeper knowledge. Name one area of your deen where you are absolutely certain, and ask yourself: Is that certainty based on evidence and reflection, or is it compound ignorance? Will you let go of false certainty to seek the truth?

The Objection of Multiple Proofs and Its Answer
The author answers the objection that multiple proofs would be impossible if nazar requires absence of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why multiple proofs can be sought even after knowing a conclusion, and the different purposes of inquiry (nazar).

Core Idea
Inquiry can be repeated for tranquility or teaching, not just for initial knowledge.

In the previous lesson, we learned that nazar requires absence of knowledge of the desired conclusion. But what if someone already knows the conclusion from one proof—can they still engage in a second proof? This passage addresses that objection by distinguishing between the purposes of inquiry. The answer shows that nazar is not restricted to acquiring new knowledge; it can also serve to increase certainty or help a learner accept the truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection and Answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the objection (beginning with 'فإن قيل') and the answer (beginning with 'أجيب').
2
Understand the Purposes of Inquiry
The answer distinguishes two additional purposes: increasing tranquility (itmi'nan) and pedagogical readiness. Notice how each purpose justifies multiple proofs.
The Imam's quote adds that the second proof examines its 'wajh dalala' (aspect of indication).
3
Reflect on the Definition of 'Result'
The passage ends with a technical definition of 'natija' (result). Consider how this definition relates to the earlier discussion about proofs.
The result is the proposition that necessarily follows from the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو كان النظر مشروطاً بعدم العلم بالمطلوب لماجاز النظر في دليل ثان وثالث على مطلوب الحصول العلم به بالدليل الأول أجيب بأن ذلك إنما يشترط حيث يقصد بالنظر طلب العلم أو الظن، لكن قد تورد صورة النظر والاستدلال لا لذلك بل لغرض آخر عائد إلى الناظر، وهو زيادة الاطمئنان بتعاضد الأدلة أو إلى المتعلم بأن يكون ممن يحصل له استعداد القبول باجتماع الأدلة دون كل واحد أو بهذا الدليل دون ذلك، فإن الأذهان مختلفة في قبول اليقين فربما يحصل للبعض من دليل ولبعض آخر من دليل آخر ، وربما يحصل من الاجتماع كما في الاقناعيات . قال الإمام : النظر في الدليل الثاني نظر في وجه دلالته أي المطلوب منه كونه دليلاً على النتيجة وهو غير معلوم والحق أن هذا لازم لكن المطلوب. والنتيجة اسم لما يلزم المقدمات بالذات وبالتعيين وهو القضية التي موضوعها موضوع الصغرى ومحمولها محمول الكبرى.
If it is said: If inquiry (nazar) were conditioned by the absence of knowledge of the desired conclusion, then it would not be permissible to engage in inquiry into a second and third proof regarding a desired conclusion when knowledge of it has already been obtained through the first proof. The answer: That condition applies only where the purpose of inquiry is to seek knowledge or opinion; but sometimes the form of inquiry and inference (istidlal) occurs not for that purpose but for another purpose returning to the inquirer, namely increased tranquility (itmi'nan) through the mutual reinforcement of proofs, or (returning) to the learner, namely that he may have the readiness of acceptance through the collection of proofs rather than through any single one, or through this proof rather than that. For minds differ in accepting certainty; perhaps some attain it from one proof and others from another, and perhaps they attain it from the combination, as in persuasive arguments (iqna'iyyat). The Imam said: Inquiry into the second proof is inquiry into the aspect of its indication (wajh dalalataha), i.e., what is sought from it is that it is a proof for the result, and that is not known. The truth is that this is necessary, but (the desired conclusion) is not sought. And 'result' (natija) is a name for what follows from the premises by essence and by specification, and it is the proposition whose subject is the subject of the minor premise and whose predicate is the predicate of the major premise.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection to the condition that nazar requires absence of knowledge of the desired conclusion. The objection: if one already knows the conclusion from a first proof, how can one legitimately consider a second or third proof? The answer distinguishes between the purpose of nazar: it may be for seeking knowledge or opinion, or for other purposes such as gaining greater tranquility (itmi'nan) through multiple proofs, or for pedagogical reasons where a learner may need multiple proofs to accept the truth. The author quotes the Imam who says that the second proof is examined for its indicational aspect (wajh dalalataha), i.e., to see whether it indeed indicates the result, which is not yet known. The author then clarifies that the 'result' (natija) is the proposition that necessarily follows from the premises.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
دليل (dalil)
اطمئنان (itmi'nan)
إقناعيات (iqna'iyyat)
وجه دلالة (wajh dalala)
نتيجة (natija)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar can only be performed once for a given conclusion; after knowing it, further inquiry is invalid.
Correction: Nazar can be repeated for increased tranquility or to help a learner accept the truth, even if the conclusion is already known from a prior proof.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it permissible to engage in inquiry into a second proof after already knowing the conclusion?
A) Because the condition of absence of knowledge applies only when seeking knowledge or opinion
B) Because the second proof is always more reliable
C) Because the first proof might be invalid
D) Because the inquirer forgets the first proof

Key Takeaway
Multiple proofs are permissible when the purpose is not initial discovery but increased certainty or pedagogical accommodation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a single proof for a belief and resisted additional evidence? What does that reveal about your heart's need for comfort versus its willingness to be challenged? This lesson calls you to examine your motives: do you seek multiple proofs for tranquility or for control? Spend a few minutes in honest self-assessment and ask Allah to purify your intention.
?
Think of a time when you presented a proof to others but they remained unconvinced. Did you consider their need for a different proof or a combination? This lesson teaches that minds differ in accepting certainty. Will you now humbly adapt your approach, seeking to guide hearts rather than merely prove a point? Commit to one specific change in how you share truth.
?
The passage mentions that the second proof is not about the result but about its indication. Are you sometimes so attached to a conclusion that you stop examining how your proofs actually indicate it? Could your certainty be masking an unwillingness to test the soundness of your reasoning? Ask Allah for honesty and courage to scrutinize your own evidence.
