Is a Teacher Required for Theological Reflection?
Al-Taftazani refutes the heretics' claim that reflecting on God requires a teacher.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani argues that a teacher is not a necessary condition for correct theological reflection about God, and how he refutes the heretics' objection.

Core Idea
Correct reflection suffices for knowing God; a teacher is helpful but not required.

This passage addresses a fundamental methodological question: can a person come to know God through their own reasoning, or must they have a teacher? Al-Taftazani firmly states that a teacher is not a condition, because correct reflection itself yields knowledge. He then anticipates and answers the heretics' objection based on the diversity of opinions and the need for teachers in other fields. The passage also begins his own proof, though it cuts off.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall flow of the argument. Notice how al-Taftazani moves from his assertion to reasons, then to objection and reply, and finally to his own proof.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main argument structure is clear.
2
Identify the claim and supporting reasons
Find al-Taftazani's initial statement and the three reasons he gives (correct reflection yields knowledge, infinite regress of teachers, and the problem of verifying the teacher's truthfulness). Understand how each reason supports the claim.
3
Analyze the heretics' objection and the response
The heretics argue from the multiplicity of opinions and the need for teachers in easy subjects. Al-Taftazani responds by distinguishing between impossibility and difficulty, and by attributing the diversity to corrupt reflections.
This is a key refutation: need in the sense of difficulty is not denied, but necessity is.
4
Examine the beginning of al-Taftazani's proof
The passage ends with the start of his first proof: that correct reflection with conditions yields knowledge. Note that this proof is incomplete, but it sets up the positive case.
You may wish to consider what conditions might be implied.
5
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this question matters: if a teacher were required, then those without access to scholars could not know God. Al-Taftazani's argument upholds the sufficiency of sound reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ولا يشترط للنظر في معرفة الله وجود المعلم. لما ثبت من إفادة النظر الصحيح العلم على الإطلاق ولأنه أيضاً يحتاج إلى معلم آخر ويتسلسل إلا أن يخص الحكم بنظر غير المعلم أو ينتهي إلى الوحي، ولأن العلم بصدقه إما بالنظر فتهافت، أو بقوله فدور، أو بآخر فتسلسل. وقد يجاب بأنه النظر المقرون بإرشاد من العلم. واحتجت الملاحدة بكثرة اختلاف الآراء في الإلهيات وتحقق الاحتياج إلى المعلم في أسهل العلوم والصناعات. والجواب: انها لكثرة الأنظار الفاسدة وأن الاحتياج بمعنى تعذر الكسب بدونه غير مسلم وبمعنى تعسره غير متنازع، إذ لا خفاء في أن الإرشاد إلى المقدمات وحل الأشكال نعم العون على تحصيل الكمالات خلافاً للملاحدة. لنا وجوه: الأول: أنه قد بينها إفادة النظر الصحيح المقرون بالشرائط العلم على ...
His saying: And it is not a condition for theoretical reflection (naẓar) in knowing God that there be a teacher (mu‘allim) present. Because it is established that correct reflection yields knowledge absolutely, and also because it would need another teacher and so on ad infinitum, unless the judgment is particularized to the reflection of someone other than the teacher, or it ends in revelation; and because the knowledge of his truthfulness is either by reflection — then it is incoherent, or by his statement — then it is circular, or by another — then it is an infinite regress. And it may be answered that it is reflection coupled with guidance (irshād) from knowledge. And the heretics (mulāḥidah) argued by the multiplicity of differing opinions in theology and the actual need for a teacher in the easiest sciences and crafts. The answer: that it is due to the multiplicity of corrupt reflections, and the need in the sense of impossibility of acquisition without it is not conceded, and in the sense of difficulty is not disputed, for there is no concealment that guidance to premises and solving difficulties is indeed an aid to the attainment of perfections, contrary to the heretics. We have proofs: The first: that we have already shown that correct reflection coupled with conditions yields knowledge...
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage addresses the claim by the heretics (mulāḥidah) that theoretical reflection (naẓar) in the knowledge of God requires a teacher (mu‘allim). Al-Taftazani begins by stating the proposition: a teacher is not a condition. He provides several reasons: First, correct reflection yields knowledge absolutely. Second, requiring a teacher would lead to an infinite regress unless the teacher's knowledge comes by other means or ends in revelation. Third, knowledge of the teacher's truthfulness would itself require reflection, leading to circularity or regress. A potential answer—that it is reflection guided by knowledge—is mentioned but not fully addressed here. The heretics object by pointing to the diversity of opinions in theology and the need for teachers in simple disciplines. Al-Taftazani responds that the diversity is due to corrupt reflections, not to the impossibility of acquiring knowledge without a teacher. The need for a teacher in the sense of 'impossible to acquire' is not conceded, only in the sense of 'difficult' is admitted. Guidance to premises and solving difficulties is indeed an aid, but not a necessary condition. Then he begins to list his own proofs, starting with the first: that we have already shown that correct reflection with conditions yields knowledge. The text cuts off at this point.

Key Terms
النظر
المعلم
الملاحدة
إفادة النظر الصحيح
التسلسل
الدور
الأنظار الفاسدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that knowing God necessarily requires a human teacher, because theological opinions are so diverse and even simple skills require instruction.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that correct reflection itself can yield knowledge, and that the diversity is due to faulty reasoning, not the absence of a teacher. Teachers are aids, not necessities.

Checkpoint
What is the heretics' main argument against the possibility of knowing God without a teacher?
A) That correct reflection never yields knowledge.
B) That there is a great diversity of opinions in theology and even simple sciences require teachers.
C) That the Qur'an prohibits reflection without a teacher.
D) That no one has ever attained knowledge of God without a teacher.

Key Takeaway
A teacher is a help, not a condition, for correct theological reflection; sound reasoning with proper conditions suffices for knowing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have a question about your faith, do you immediately look for a scholar's answer, or do you engage your own mind in reflection on the evidence? What does this lesson say about relying on your own reflection? How can you balance seeking guidance with personal reflection?
?
The heretics argued that the multiplicity of differing opinions in theology shows the need for a teacher. But Al-Taftazani says the multiplicity is due to corrupt reflections. How often do you accept opinions without checking if they are based on correct reflection? Are you willing to think for yourself, even if it means disagreeing with popular views?
?
This lesson distinguishes between impossibility and difficulty in acquiring knowledge. There is a difference between 'cannot' and 'it's hard'. In your own pursuit of knowledge about God, do you sometimes use 'difficulty' as an excuse to give up? What would it take for you to persist in reflection despite the challenges?

The Thesis and the Mulahida's Objection
The commentator states the thesis and responds to an initial objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial thesis that reflection does not require a teacher, the three brief proofs summarized, and the objection of the Mulahida with its response.

Core Idea
Correct reflection with proper conditions is fully sufficient for knowledge; a teacher is not necessary.

This lesson introduces the core thesis of the commentator: that rational reflection on God does not depend on a teacher. The passage begins by asserting this claim, quickly summarizes three logical proofs, and then addresses a key objection from those who deny it. As you read, notice how the argument sets the stage for a detailed defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening thesis and summary of proofs
Notice how the commentator states the thesis and gives three quick arguments: infinite regress, circularity, and regress in verifying truthfulness.
These are compressed, anticipating the detailed proofs later.
2
Examine the potential answer and the Mulahida objection
The commentator mentions a possible answer (reflection with guidance) and then presents the atheists' objection from disagreement and need for teachers in other fields.
3
Focus on the response
The commentator refutes the objection by distinguishing between impossibility (not needed) and difficulty (acknowledged).
This response clears the way for the detailed proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله : ولا يشترط للنظر في معرفة الله وجود المعلم) . لما ثبت من إفادة النظر الصحيح العلم على الإطلاق (۳) ولأنه أيضاً يحتاج إلى معلم آخر ويتسلسل إلا أن يخص الحكم بنظر غير المعلم أو ينتهي إلى الوحي ، ولأن العلم بصدقه إما بالنظر فتهافت، أو بقوله فدور، أو بآخر فتسلسل . وقد يجاب بأنه النظر المقرون بإرشاد من العلم. واحتجت الملاحدة بكثرة اختلاف الآراء في الإلهيات وتحقق الاحتياج إلى المعلم في أسهل العلوم والصناعات . والجواب : انها لكثرة الأنظار الفاسدة () وأن الاحتياج بمعنى تعذر الكسب بدونه غير مسلم وبمعنى تعسره غير متنازع، إذ لا خفاء في أن الإرشاد إلى المقدمات وحل الأشكال نعم العون على تحصيل الكمالات خلافاً للملاحدة. لنا وجوه : الأول : أنه قد بينها إفادة النظر الصحيح المقرون بالشرائط العلم على الإطلاق، سواء كان في المعارف الإلهية أو غيرها وسواء كان معه معلم أولا . وأما إمكان تحصيل المقدمات الضرورية وترتيبها على الوجه المنتج لأنه صناعة المنطق فمعلوم بالضرورة. الثاني : أن نظر المعلم أيضاً لكونه نظر إلى معرفة الله تعالى يحتاج إلى معرفة معلم آخر ويتسلسل، إلا أن يخص الاحتياج إلى المعلم بغير المعلم، ويجعل نظر المعلم كافياً لكونه مخصوصاً بتأييد إلهي ، أو تنتهي سلسلة التعليم إلى المعلم المستند علمه إلى النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام المستند إلى الوحي . الثالث: أن إرشاد المعلم لا يفيدنا إلا بعد العلم بصدقه، وصدقه إما أن يعلم بالنظر فيكون النظر كافيا في المعرفة، حيث أفاد صدق المعلم المفيد للمعرفة وإما أن يعلم بقول ذلك المعلم فيدور لأن قوله أي إخباره عن كونه صادقاً لا يفيد كونه كذلك إلا بعد العلم بأنه صادق البتة، وإما بقول معلم آخر وهكذا إلى أن يتسلسل، وقد يجاب بأنا لا نجعل المعلم مستقلاً بإفادة المعرفة ليلزمنا العلم بكونه صادقاً لا يكذب البتة بل نجعل المفيد هو النظر المقرون بإرشاد منه إلى الأدلة ودفع الشبهة، لكون عقولنا قاصرة عن الاستدلال بذلك مفتقرة إلى إمام يعلمنا الأدلة ودفع الشبه ليحصل لنا بواسطة تعليمه وقوة عقولنا معرفة الحقائق الإلهية التي من جملتها كونه إماماً يستحق الإرشاد والتعليم ثم لا يخفى أن ما ذكر من الوجوه بتقدير تمامها إنما يراد الاحتياج إلى المعلم في حصول المعرفة وأما لو أرادوا الاحتياج إليه في حصول النجاة بمعنى
(His statement: 'The existence of a teacher is not a condition for reflection (nazar) in knowing God.') Because it has been established that correct reflection leads to knowledge absolutely, and also because otherwise it would need another teacher leading to infinite regress unless the ruling is restricted to the reflection of a non-teacher or it ends in revelation, and because knowledge of his truthfulness is either through reflection – then it is contradictory, or through his statement – then it is circular, or through another – then it leads to infinite regress. And it may be answered that it is reflection coupled with guidance from knowledge. The Mulahida argued by the abundance of disagreement of opinions in theology and the proven need for a teacher in the easiest sciences and crafts. The answer: it is due to the abundance of corrupt reflections, and the need in the sense of the impossibility of acquisition without it is not granted, and in the sense of difficulty it is not disputed, for there is no doubt that guidance to premises and solving difficulties is an excellent aid to attaining perfections, contrary to the Mulahida. We have proofs: First: it has been demonstrated that correct reflection coupled with conditions produces knowledge absolutely, whether in divine sciences or others, and whether with a teacher or not. As for the possibility of obtaining necessary premises and ordering them in a productive manner, because it is the science of logic, it is known necessarily. Second: the teacher's own reflection, since it is reflection on knowing God, also needs another teacher and so on to infinity, unless the need for a teacher is restricted to non-teachers and the teacher's reflection is considered sufficient because it is specifically supported by divine aid, or the chain of teaching ends with a teacher whose knowledge is based on the Prophet, peace be upon him, which is based on revelation. Third: the teacher's guidance does not benefit us except after knowing his truthfulness. His truthfulness is either known through reflection – then reflection is sufficient in knowledge, since it yields knowledge of the teacher's truthfulness which yields knowledge, or it is known by that teacher's statement – then it is circular, because his statement, i.e., his informing about being truthful, does not give that he is truthful except after knowing that he is, indeed, truthful, or by another teacher's statement, and so on to infinity. And it may be answered that we do not make the teacher independent in imparting knowledge such that we need to know his absolute truthfulness; rather, we make the beneficial thing the reflection coupled with his guidance to evidences and refutation of doubts, because our intellects are deficient in independent deduction and need an imam who teaches us evidences and refutes doubts so that, through his teaching and the strength of our intellects, we may obtain knowledge of the divine realities, which include that he is an imam deserving guidance and teaching. Then it is not hidden that what has been mentioned of the proofs, assuming their completeness, is intended to show the need for a teacher in attaining knowledge, but if they intended the need for him in attaining salvation...
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is an argument that rational reflection (nazar) does not require a teacher to attain knowledge of God, contrary to the Mulahida (atheists/materialists). The commentator provides three proofs. First, correct reflection with its conditions yields knowledge universally. The ability to obtain necessary premises and arrange them logically is self-evident. Second, if a teacher were needed, the teacher himself would need another teacher, leading to infinite regress, unless one exempts the teacher or ends the chain with a prophet. Third, a teacher's guidance only benefits after verifying his truthfulness, which itself would require either reflection (making the teacher redundant) or another teacher (leading to regress) or the teacher's own word (circular). A potential answer to the third proof is that the teacher is not an independent source but a guide, and reflection assisted by his guidance suffices. The passage concludes by noting that these proofs address the necessity of a teacher for knowledge, not necessarily for salvation.

Key Terms
النظر
المعلم
الملاحدة
التسلسل
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a teacher is needed for other sciences, it must be needed for theology.
Correction: The commentator argues that the need in other sciences is for difficulty, not impossibility, and correct reflection suffices for theology.

Checkpoint
What is the Mulahida's main objection?
A) Disagreement in theology proves the need for a teacher.
B) Teachers are unavailable for everyone.
C) Reflection is impossible without a guide.
D) The Quran explicitly requires a teacher.

Key Takeaway
The thesis that a teacher is not required for correct reflection is defended by summarizing logical arguments and refuting the objection from disagreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on a teacher or scholar to tell you about God without personally reflecting on the evidence yourself? What would it take for you to begin using your own intellect—with proper premises—to reach certainty?
?
When you see disagreements among Muslims about theological issues, do you feel discouraged from seeking the truth yourself? How can you distinguish between 'corrupt reflections' and sound reasoning in your own search?
?
Are you willing to admit that sometimes you avoid deep reflection because it is difficult? What specific excuse do you use most often, and what will you do this week to overcome it, knowing that difficulty is not impossibility?

The Three Detailed Proofs
The commentator elaborates three proofs demonstrating that a teacher is not necessary for reflection on God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three detailed proofs that correct reflection without a teacher is sufficient: the first proof (correct reflection yields knowledge absolutely), the second proof (infinite regress if a teacher were needed), and the third proof (circularity/regress in verifying a teacher's truthfulness).

Core Idea
Each proof shows that requiring a teacher leads to a logical problem: regress or circularity.

In this lesson, we examine the commentator's three detailed proofs that build on the initial thesis. The first proof emphasizes the self-sufficiency of correct reflection, the second exposes the infinite regress of requiring teachers, and the third reveals the circularity in relying on a teacher's word. A potential answer to the third proof is also discussed.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Notice how it claims correct reflection with conditions yields knowledge universally, whether in divine sciences or others, and with or without a teacher.
This proof relies on the self-evident nature of logic.
2
Read the second proof
See how the teacher's own reflection would need another teacher, leading to infinite regress, unless a special exception is made.
3
Read the third proof
Understand that verifying the teacher's truthfulness leads to either circularity or regress.
4
Examine the answer to the third proof
The commentator explains that reflection coupled with the teacher's guidance suffices; the teacher is not independent.
This answer reconciles the need for guidance with the sufficiency of reflection.

(His statement: 'The existence of a teacher is not a condition for reflection (nazar) in knowing God.') Because it has been established that correct reflection leads to knowledge absolutely, and also because otherwise it would need another teacher leading to infinite regress unless the ruling is restricted to the reflection of a non-teacher or it ends in revelation, and because knowledge of his truthfulness is either through reflection – then it is contradictory, or through his statement – then it is circular, or through another – then it leads to infinite regress. And it may be answered that it is reflection coupled with guidance from knowledge. The Mulahida argued by the abundance of disagreement of opinions in theology and the proven need for a teacher in the easiest sciences and crafts. The answer: it is due to the abundance of corrupt reflections, and the need in the sense of the impossibility of acquisition without it is not granted, and in the sense of difficulty it is not disputed, for there is no doubt that guidance to premises and solving difficulties is an excellent aid to attaining perfections, contrary to the Mulahida. We have proofs: First: it has been demonstrated that correct reflection coupled with conditions produces knowledge absolutely, whether in divine sciences or others, and whether with a teacher or not. As for the possibility of obtaining necessary premises and ordering them in a productive manner, because it is the science of logic, it is known necessarily. Second: the teacher's own reflection, since it is reflection on knowing God, also needs another teacher and so on to infinity, unless the need for a teacher is restricted to non-teachers and the teacher's reflection is considered sufficient because it is specifically supported by divine aid, or the chain of teaching ends with a teacher whose knowledge is based on the Prophet, peace be upon him, which is based on revelation. Third: the teacher's guidance does not benefit us except after knowing his truthfulness. His truthfulness is either known through reflection – then reflection is sufficient in knowledge, since it yields knowledge of the teacher's truthfulness which yields knowledge, or it is known by that teacher's statement – then it is circular, because his statement, i.e., his informing about being truthful, does not give that he is truthful except after knowing that he is, indeed, truthful, or by another teacher's statement, and so on to infinity. And it may be answered that we do not make the teacher independent in imparting knowledge such that we need to know his absolute truthfulness; rather, we make the beneficial thing the reflection coupled with his guidance to evidences and refutation of doubts, because our intellects are deficient in independent deduction and need an imam who teaches us evidences and refutes doubts so that, through his teaching and the strength of our intellects, we may obtain knowledge of the divine realities, which include that he is an imam deserving guidance and teaching. Then it is not hidden that what has been mentioned of the proofs, assuming their completeness, is intended to show the need for a teacher in attaining knowledge, but if they intended the need for him in attaining salvation...
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is an argument that rational reflection (nazar) does not require a teacher to attain knowledge of God, contrary to the Mulahida (atheists/materialists). The commentator provides three proofs. First, correct reflection with its conditions yields knowledge universally. The ability to obtain necessary premises and arrange them logically is self-evident. Second, if a teacher were needed, the teacher himself would need another teacher, leading to infinite regress, unless one exempts the teacher or ends the chain with a prophet. Third, a teacher's guidance only benefits after verifying his truthfulness, which itself would require either reflection (making the teacher redundant) or another teacher (leading to regress) or the teacher's own word (circular). A potential answer to the third proof is that the teacher is not an independent source but a guide, and reflection assisted by his guidance suffices. The passage concludes by noting that these proofs address the necessity of a teacher for knowledge, not necessarily for salvation.

Key Terms
النظر
المعلم
التسلسل
الدور
الإرشاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third proof implies teachers are completely useless.
Correction: The answer clarifies that teachers are not necessary for the reflection itself, but their guidance assists due to human intellectual deficiency.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical problem raised in the second proof?
A) Circular reasoning
B) Infinite regress
C) Contradiction
D) Lack of evidence

Key Takeaway
The three proofs demonstrate that requiring a teacher leads to logical contradictions; correct reflection is inherently sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about your faith, do you verify the teacher's truthfulness for yourself, or do you accept it blindly? This passage shows that blind acceptance leads to circular reasoning. What step will you take today to reflect on a teaching you have taken for granted?
?
The first proof says correct reflection yields knowledge absolutely. Have you ever truly reflected on the existence of God using your own intellect, or have you relied entirely on others? Commit to spending ten minutes this week in solitary reflection on one of God's signs.
?
The response to the third proof acknowledges that our intellects are deficient and need guidance. Yet the proofs show that a teacher cannot be the ultimate source. How do you personally balance seeking knowledge from scholars while still exercising your own reason? Write down one area where you will stop outsourcing thinking and start reflecting yourself.

Three Objections to Needing a Teacher and Their Answer
Author lists three objections to needing a teacher for divine knowledge and answers them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational objections to the necessity of a teacher for acquiring divine knowledge and the theological response that defends the teacher's role as a guide for rational reflection.

Core Idea
A teacher is not a conveyor of knowledge but a guide for the student's own rational reflection.

This passage presents three powerful objections against the claim that a teacher is necessary for knowing God. Each objection raises a logical difficulty: if basic premises are known by necessity, if the teacher himself would need a teacher, or if the teacher's truthfulness cannot be established without circularity or infinite regress. Sanusi answers all three by clarifying the teacher's actual function as a facilitator of the student's own reasoning, not an independent source of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections
Read the passage carefully and identify the three distinct objections against the necessity of a teacher for divine knowledge. Note how each objection targets a different aspect of the teacher's role.
The first objection is implied in the opening statement about absolute ability and necessary premises.
2
Understand the answer
Notice that the answer at the end ('وقد يجاب...') addresses the objections by redefining the teacher's role. The teacher is not independent; rather, the student's own reflection combined with the teacher's guidance leads to knowledge.
The answer rejects the assumption that the teacher must be an independent source of knowledge.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider how each objection is resolved by the general answer. For the first, the capacity for logic is innate, but guidance is still needed. For the second, the teacher's knowledge is not independent but supported by divine aid or prophetic chain. For the third, we do not need to verify the teacher's truthfulness independently because the teacher is not the source of knowledge.
4
Connect to methodology
See how this passage fits into the broader kalam methodology: rational reflection is primary, but it is guided by authoritative teachers (imams) who provide proofs and remove doubts.
This reflects the balance between reason and authority in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإطلاق، سواء كان في المعارف الإلهية أو غيرها وسواء كان معه معلم أولا . وأما إمكان تحصيل المقدمات الضرورية وترتيبها على الوجه المنتج لأنه صناعة المنطق فمعلوم بالضرورة. الثاني : أن نظر المعلم أيضاً لكونه نظر إلى معرفة الله تعالى يحتاج إلى معرفة معلم آخر ويتسلسل، إلا أن يخص الاحتياج إلى المعلم بغير المعلم، ويجعل نظر المعلم كافياً لكونه مخصوصاً بتأييد إلهي ، أو تنتهي سلسلة التعليم إلى المعلم المستند علمه إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم المستند إلى الوحي . الثالث: أن إرشاد المعلم لا يفيدنا إلا بعد العلم بصدقه، وصدقه إما أن يعلم بالنظر فيكون النظر كافيا في المعرفة، حيث أفاد صدق المعلم المفيد للمعرفة وإما أن يعلم بقول ذلك المعلم فيدور لأن قوله أي إخباره عن كونه صادقاً لا يفيد كونه كذلك إلا بعد العلم بأنه صادق البتة، وإما بقول معلم آخر وهكذا إلى أن يتسلسل، وقد يجاب بأنا لا نجعل المعلم مستقلاً بإفادة المعرفة ليلزمنا العلم بكونه صادقاً لا يكذب البتة بل نجعل المفيد هو النظر المقرون بإرشاد منه إلى الأدلة ودفع الشبهة، لكون عقولنا قاصرة عن الاستدلال بذلك مفتقرة إلى إمام يعلمنا الأدلة ودفع الشبه ليحصل لنا بواسطة تعليمه وقوة عقولنا معرفة الحقائق الإلهية التي من جملتها كونه إماماً يستحق الإرشاد والتعليم
Absolute [ability], whether in divine knowledge or otherwise, and whether with a teacher or without. As for the possibility of acquiring necessary premises and arranging them in a productive manner, because it is the science of logic, it is known by necessity. Second: That the teacher's reflection, because it is reflection on knowledge of God Almighty, needs knowledge of another teacher and so on in a chain, unless the need for a teacher is specified for someone other than the teacher, and the teacher's reflection is considered sufficient because it is supported by divine aid, or the chain of teaching ends with a teacher whose knowledge is based on the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) based on revelation. Third: That the teacher's guidance does not benefit us except after knowing his truthfulness, and his truthfulness is either known by reflection, then reflection is sufficient for knowledge, since it has shown the truthfulness of the teacher who provides knowledge, or it is known by that teacher's statement, which is circular because his statement—i.e., his informing about his being truthful—does not establish that he is such except after knowing that he is absolutely truthful, or by another teacher's statement, and so on ad infinitum. And it may be answered that we do not consider the teacher as independent in conveying knowledge so that we would need to know that he is truthful and never lies; rather, we consider the beneficial factor to be reflection combined with his guidance toward proofs and removal of doubts, because our intellects are incapable of reasoning by themselves and need an imam to teach us the proofs and remove doubts, so that through his teaching and the strength of our intellects we may obtain knowledge of the divine realities, including that he is an imam worthy of guidance and teaching.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the necessity of a teacher for acquiring divine knowledge and then answers them. The first objection (implicitly starting with 'الإطلاق') asserts that the basic premises of reasoning are known by necessity, so a teacher is not needed. The second objection argues that if a teacher himself needs to have knowledge of God, he would need another teacher, leading to infinite regress, unless the teacher is divinely aided or traces back to a prophet. The third objection raises the issue of the teacher's truthfulness: we cannot know he is truthful without prior knowledge, leading to circularity or infinite regress. The answer rejects the assumption that the teacher is an independent source of knowledge. Instead, the teacher's role is to guide the student's own rational reflection, providing proofs and removing doubts. The student's intellect, though weak, can then attain knowledge. The teacher is thus an imam who facilitates the student's own reasoning.

Key Terms
معلم
نظر
معرفة
تسلسل
إرشاد
إمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The teacher is a passive conveyor of information whose truthfulness must be independently verified before any learning can occur.
Correction: The teacher is not an independent conveyor of knowledge but a facilitator. The student's own rational reflection, guided by the teacher, is the true means of knowledge. The teacher's truthfulness is not a prerequisite because knowledge is not received from the teacher but attained through shared reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the third objection against needing a teacher?
A) The teacher's knowledge must come from a prophet.
B) The teacher's truthfulness cannot be known without circularity or infinite regress.
C) The teacher must be supported by divine aid.
D) The teacher's reflection is sufficient for knowledge.

Key Takeaway
A teacher is necessary not as an independent source of knowledge, but as a guide who activates and directs the student's own rational reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge about God, do you honestly rely on your own reasoning without seeking guidance from those more learned? What might be the cost of such pride?
?
Think of a doubt you have struggled with in your faith. Did you seek out a teacher to help remove that doubt, or did you try to resolve it alone? What does that choice reveal about your understanding of the teacher's role?
?
Is there an area of your religious practice where you have assumed you can figure it out yourself, ignoring the need for a qualified guide? Will you commit today to finding such a guide, humbling yourself before the process?

The Obligation of Nazar: Consensus and Two Positions
Presents the consensus on nazar and the two views on its basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all Muslims agree on the obligation of nazar for knowing God, but they differ on whether this obligation is grounded in revelation or reason.

Core Idea
The obligation to reflect is agreed; its source is disputed.

This lesson introduces the fourth investigation of the text, which addresses a foundational issue in Islamic theology: Is rational inquiry (nazar) for knowing God obligatory, and if so, what makes it so? While all Muslims agree on its obligation, they disagree sharply on the basis—whether it is revealed law (shar') or pure reason ('aql). The text presents the Ash'ari position and refutes the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Begin by reading the first sentence which affirms universal agreement on the obligation of nazar. Note how it frames nazar as a possible prerequisite (muqaddima maqdūra) for obligatory knowledge.
This sets the stage for the debate.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice the contrast between 'as for us' (Ash'aris) and 'according to the Mu'tazila'. Mark how each camp grounds the obligation differently: revelation vs. reason.
The inline numbers refer to footnotes not included here.
3
Study the refutation
Read the Ash'ari rebuttal against the Mu'tazila rational argument. Observe the two points: denial of fear in most cases, and the counterexample of children and the insane.
This refutation uses common-sense examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع: لا خلاف بين أهل الإسلام في وجوب النظر في معرفة الله تعالى لكونه مقدمة مقدورة للمعرفة الواجبة مطلقاً، أما عندنا (1) فبالشرع (2) بالنص والإجماع (3) إذ حكم العقل معزول لما سيجـ عند المعتزلة فعقلاً لكونها دافعة لضرر خوف العقاب وغيره، ورد بمنع الخوف في الأغلب لعدم الشعور ، ولو سلم فالخوف بحالة لاحتمال الخطأ وكون العارف أحسن حالاً ليس على إطلاقه بل البلاهة أدنى إلى الخلاص، كما في الصبي ،والمجنون وقد ينازع في إمكان إيجاب المعرفة لما فيه من تحصيل الحاصل أو تكليف الغافل، وفي الإجماع على وجوبها، فلقد كانوا يكتفون بالتقليد والانقياد وفي أن النظر مقدمها فقد تحصل يمثل التعليم والإلهام، وفي إطلاق وجوبها إذ هو مقيد بالشك أو عدم المعرفة، وفي وجوب المقدمة لجواز إيجاب الأصل مع الذهول عنها، فيجاب بأنه لا غفلة مع فهم الخطاب،
The fourth investigation: There is no disagreement among the people of Islam regarding the obligation of reflection (nazar) for knowing God Almighty, because it is a possible prerequisite for the absolutely obligatory knowledge. As for us (1), it is by the Law (2) through the text and consensus (3), since the judgment of reason is isolated [in this matter] as will come. According to the Mu'tazila, it is by reason, because it repels the harm of fear of punishment and other things. It is refuted by denying fear in most cases due to lack of awareness. Even if granted, fear is [still] a condition due to the possibility of error, and that the knower is in a better state is not absolute, rather ignorance is closer to salvation, as in the case of the child and the insane. And there is dispute over the possibility of making knowledge obligatory because it would entail obtaining something already obtained or obligating the heedless; and over consensus on its obligation, for they used to be content with imitation and submission; and over the fact that reflection is its prerequisite, for it might be obtained through teaching, inspiration; and over the absoluteness of its obligation, for it is conditioned by doubt or lack of knowledge; and over the obligation of the prerequisite, because it is permissible to make the principal obligator while heedless of it. It is answered that there is no heedlessness with understanding of the address.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage opens the fourth investigation (mabḥath) of the text. It begins by stating the unanimous agreement among all Muslims on the obligation of rational inquiry (naẓar) for acquiring knowledge of God, since such inquiry is a possible means to that obligatory knowledge. The passage then identifies the major point of disagreement: the source of this obligation. For the Ash'aris (Ahl al-Sunna), the obligation is grounded in revealed Law (sharʿ) via scriptural texts and consensus (ijmāʿ), because the judgment of reason is set aside in this matter (a claim that will be explained later). For the Mu'tazila, the obligation is rational (ʿaqlī), because inquiry repels the harm (ḍarar) of fear of punishment and other consequences. The author then gives an Ash'arī response: first, most people are unaware of the threat of punishment, so fear is absent; even if fear exists, the ignorant person may be safer, as with children and the insane. The passage then enumerates several objections to the Ash'arī position: 1) making knowledge obligatory might involve tautology or burdening the heedless; 2) consensus on obligation is contested, since early Muslims were content with blind imitation (taqlīd); 3) reflection is not a necessary prerequisite, since knowledge can be obtained through teaching, inspiration, etc.; 4) the obligation is not absolute but conditional on doubt or lack of knowledge; 5) the prerequisite itself is not obligatory if the principal obligation can be fulfilled without awareness. The passage concludes with a brief answer: there is no heedlessness when one understands the address (i.e., the divine command to know).

Key Terms
نظر
وجوب
شرع
عقل
خوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the passage denies the role of reason entirely.
Correction: Rather, it limits the authority of reason in grounding obligation; reason is still used within revelation's framework.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arīs, what is the basis for the obligation of naẓar?
A) Reason (ʿaql)
B) Revelation (sharʿ)
C) Consensus of all Muslims
D) Fear of punishment

Key Takeaway
The obligation of nazar is universally accepted, but its source is revelation for Ash'aris and reason for Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage argues that the obligation to reflect on God comes from revelation, not merely from fear of punishment. Have you ever pursued knowledge of God merely to escape fear—or have you sought it because you recognize the duty placed on you by the One who revealed Himself? Which motive drives your current pursuit of faith? Take a moment to honestly assess your intention and ask God to purify it.
?
The refutation says that the ignorant may be in a safer state (like children and the insane). Does this comfort you when you fail to understand a difficult theological point—or does it expose your own laziness in seeking knowledge? Will you allow this possibility to excuse inaction, or will it spur you to reflect more deeply because you know the obligation is upon you?
?
The Mu‘tazila argued that nazar is necessary to remove the harm of punishment. If you were suddenly convinced that no punishment awaits you, would you still engage in reflection to know God? Examine your heart: is your love for God and your sense of His claim over you strong enough to sustain the search even without fear? If not, what does that reveal about the foundation of your faith?

Objections to the Ash'ari Position and Their Reply
Enumerates five objections to the Ash'ari position and gives a unified reply

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against the Ash'ari position on the obligation of nazar and the concise reply to them.

Core Idea
The obligation of nazar faces five objections, each answered by: no heedlessness when the divine address is understood.

After establishing the Ash'ari position that nazar is obligatory by revelation, the text anticipates several objections. These objections challenge the logical coherence of making nazar obligatory: Could it involve tautology? Was consensus truly universal? Is nazar the only path to knowledge? Is the obligation absolute? Is a prerequisite always necessary? The answer ties everything to the understanding of the divine command.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections carefully
The passage lists five objections starting with 'and there is dispute over...'. Identify each one by its subject: possibility, consensus, precedence, absoluteness, and prerequisite.
These are presented as challenges to the Ash'ari view.
2
Understand the first objection
The objection about impossibility: if knowledge already exists, it's redundant; if not, it's burdening the heedless. Consider why this is a problem.
This is a logical dilemma (tahsil al-hasil or taklif al-ghafil).
3
Notice the pattern of objections
Each objection targets a different aspect: the very possibility of obligation, the consensus claim, the necessity of nazar, the unconditional nature, and the prerequisite obligation.
All five aim to undermine the Ash'ari position.
4
Study the answer
The reply is a single statement: 'there is no heedlessness with understanding of the address.' Reflect on how this resolves all five objections.
The answer asserts that the divine command itself removes the excuse of ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The reply says there is no heedlessness when you understand the divine address. When you hear a verse or hadith commanding something, do you immediately feel the weight of that command on your soul, or do you let it slide by as mere information? What would it take for you to never be able to claim heedlessness again?
?

?

Which command of Allah are you most likely to ignore, and what story do you tell yourself to justify that neglect? Will you stop telling that story starting today?

Defending Reflection Against Objections
Taftazani answers objections to wajib al-nazar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections against requiring reflection for knowledge of God and how Taftazani refutes them, establishing that reflection is a necessary cause.

Core Idea
Reflection is the necessary cause for knowledge of God, and its obligation is absolute.

After establishing that knowledge of God is obligatory, Taftazani now addresses objections that might suggest knowledge can be obtained without personal reflection. This passage shows why even indirect means of learning still depend on a core reflective act, and why the obligation to reflect is not negated by exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to get the overall flow of the argument.
The passage ends abruptly; the main points are complete.
2
Identify the first objection about general proofs
Notice the objection: if consensus on obligation is mutawātir, then we could simply rely on that consensus without reflecting. Taftazani responds that permission for some to derive blessings does not negate general obligation.
3
Understand the response about absolute obligation
Taftazani argues that 'absolute obligation' means the obligation is not tied to a specific prerequisite, using analogies with fasting and pilgrimage. Focus on how he defends that non-necessary means still require some necessary reflection.
4
Analyze the claim that knowledge obligates its cause
Taftazani states that making knowledge obligatory means making its cause (reflection) obligatory, similar to how killing obligates cutting the neck. Consider why this is a strong argument.
5
See the conclusion on reflection and knowledge
He concludes that reflection is clearly possible, knowledge is theoretical (dependent on reflection), and the obligation of knowledge is either legal (our view) or rational (Mu'tazila), but in both cases it requires reflection.
The passage ends with a fragment about fear of punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإجماع على وجوب المعرفة متواتر الاكتفاء (۱) إنما كان بالأدلة الإجمالية على أن جواز التبرك للبعض لا ينافي الوجوب في الجملة (٢) واحتياج طرق تحصيل غير الضروري إلى نظر ما ضروري إذ الحكم مختص بالأغلب ومعنى إطلاق الوجوب عدم تقيده بتلك المقدمة، كوجوب الصوم بالنسبة إلى النية والإقامة والحج بالنسبة إلى الإحرام والاستطاعة والمعرفة كيفية لا معنى لإيجابها سوى إيجاب تحصيلها وفيه إيجاب سببها قطعاً، وكحز الرقبة في إيجاب القتل ولـو اكتفينا بالإجماع على وجوبه لكفينا هذه المؤونات . أي لأجل حصولها بقدر الطاقة البشرية لأنه أمر مقدور يتوقف عليه الواجب المطلق الذي ،المعرفة وكل مقدور يتوقف عليه الواجب المطلق فهو واجب شرعاً إذ كان وجوب الواجب) (۳) . المطلق شرعياً كما هو ديناً أو عقلاً، إن كان عقلياً كما هو رأي المعتزلة لئلا يلزم تكليف المحال، أما كون النظر مقدوراً فظاهر، وأما توقف المعرفة عليه فلأنها ليست بضرورية بل نظرية - ولا معنى للنظري إلا ما يتوقف على النظر ويتحصل به وأما وجوب المعرفة فعندنا بالشرع للنصوص الواردة فيه والإجماع المنعقد عليه واستناد جميع الواجبات إليه وعند المعتزلة بالعقل لأنها دافعة لضرر الظنون وهو خوف العقاب في الآخرة حيث أخبر جمع كثير بذلك، وخوف جمع
And the consensus on the obligation of knowledge is mutawātir, [the objection about] sufficing with general proofs [is answered] because permission for some to derive blessing does not negate obligation in general. And the means of acquisition that are non-necessary need some necessary reflection, since the ruling is specific to the majority, and the meaning of absolute obligation is that it is not restricted by that prerequisite, like the obligation of fasting regarding intention and residence, and pilgrimage regarding iḥrām and capability. And knowledge is a quality; there is no meaning to making it obligatory except making its acquisition obligatory, and that necessarily involves making its cause obligatory, like cutting the neck in making killing obligatory. If we were to rely on consensus on its obligation, it would suffice us these troubles. That is, for obtaining it to the extent of human capacity, because it is a possible thing on which the absolute obligatory depends. And every possible thing on which the absolute obligatory depends is obligatory by Law, since the obligation of the absolute obligatory is legal as it is religious, or rational if it is rational as is the view of the Mu'tazila, lest impossible burden result. As for the fact that reflection is possible, it is clear. As for the dependence of knowledge on it, because it is not necessary but rather theoretical—and there is no meaning to the theoretical except what depends on reflection and is obtained through it. As for the obligation of knowledge, according to us it is by Law due to the texts on it and the consensus established upon it and the dependence of all obligations on it; according to the Mu'tazila it is by reason because it repels the harm of conjectures, which is the fear of punishment in the Hereafter, where a large group has informed of that, and fear of a group.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage continues the argument from the previous lesson, now providing more detailed responses to the objections raised. It begins by asserting that the consensus on the obligation of knowledge is transmitted by tawātur (mass transmission), and the objection about being content with general proofs is answered because the fact that some people are allowed to derive blessings does not negate the obligation in general. Then it addresses the objection that non-necessary means (such as teaching or inspiration) do not require reflection: the response is that all such means actually need some form of essential reflection, since the ruling applies to the majority. The meaning of 'absolute obligation' is that it is not tied to that specific prerequisite—similar to how fasting is obligatory without requiring intention and residence in every case, or pilgrimage without requiring iḥrām and capability in every aspect. Knowledge is a quality: obligating it means obligating its acquisition, which necessarily obligates its cause just as cutting the neck is obligated when killing is obligated. The passage then considers whether consensus alone would suffice to prove the obligation, but it says that if we accepted consensus, it would spare us these troubles. It explains that the obligation is for it to be obtained within human capacity, because it is a possible thing that the absolute obligatory depends on, and every possible thing that the absolute obligatory depends on is itself obligatory by Law—whether the absolute obligatory is legal or rational. The last part restates the position: reflection is clearly possible; knowledge depends on it because it is not necessary but theoretical; the obligation of knowledge is legal for the Ash'aris, rational for the Mu'tazila because it repels the harm of fear of punishment, as reported by many. The passage ends abruptly with 'وخوف جمع' (and fear of a group).

Correction: Taftazani acknowledges that some means (like teaching) are valid but argues they still depend on a core reflective act.

Refuting the Objection: Knowledge Requires Reflection Even Through Teaching
Sanusi refutes the objection that knowledge can occur without nazar by showing that all alternative methods incorporate reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why even knowledge acquired through teaching, inspiration, or spiritual purification still requires reflective inquiry (nazar).

Core Idea
Every path to knowledge ultimately depends on reflection.

The objection claimed that knowledge can occur without nazar because it can be obtained through teaching, inspiration, etc. Sanusi responds by showing that all these methods implicitly involve nazar. This is a key point in the kalam methodology, defending the necessity of rational reflection for sound belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author structures his answer.
2
Identify the alternative methods
List the methods mentioned by the objection that supposedly bypass nazar: teaching, inspiration, the statement of the infallible, and spiritual struggle.
These are the targets of the refutation.
3
Understand the general response
Sanusi argues that all these methods need 'essential reflection' (نظر ضروري). Grasp why he says this—because each involves a process of reasoning.
The key is that the learner must move from premises to conclusion.
4
Focus on the teaching example
The teacher guides the student to the premises (مقدمات), the direction of evidence (وجه الدلالة), and how to repel doubts. Once the student grasps these, he moves to the conclusion—this movement is itself reflection.
The act of moving from evidence to conclusion is nazar.
5
Apply the same logic to inspiration
Inspiration cannot be trusted until it is verified that it comes from God. Such verification requires reflection.
This shows that no method is free from nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجاب عن الثالث. وهو أن المعرفة لا تتوقف على النظر حتى يجب بوجوبها لحصولها بالتعليم أو الإلهام أو قول المعصوم أو المجاهدة والتصفية، فأجاب بأن كل هذه الأشياء تحتاج إلى نظر ضروري، لأن المعلم يرشد إلى المقدمات وإلى وجه الدلالة وإلى وجه دفع الشبه والشكوك، فإذا حصل عند المتعلم ما أرشد إليه انتقل إلى النتيجة والانتقال إلى النتيجة بعد إدراك الدليل حصول عمن نظر فلم يخل التعلم عن نظر ما بهذا الاعتبار. وكذا الإلهام لا يوثق به حتى يعلم أنه من الله تعالى ولا يعلم أنه منه إلا بالنظر.
And it is answered concerning the third [objection], which is that knowledge does not depend on reflection such that it is obligated by its obligation, because it can be obtained through teaching, or inspiration, or the statement of the infallible, or struggle and purification. He answered that all these things need essential reflection, because the teacher guides to the premises and to the aspect of indication and to the aspect of repelling doubts and suspicions. Then when what he guided to is obtained by the learner, he moves to the conclusion, and the movement to the conclusion after grasping the evidence is the attainment by one who has reflected, so learning is not devoid of some reflection in this respect. Likewise, inspiration is not relied upon until it is known that it is from God Almighty, and it is not known that it is from Him except through reflection.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a detailed answer to the third objection mentioned earlier: that knowledge does not require reflective inquiry (naẓar) because it can be acquired through teaching, inspiration, the words of an infallible imam, or spiritual struggle and purification. The author refutes this by arguing that all these methods ultimately rely on a necessary core of reflection. In the case of teaching, the teacher guides the student to the premises (muqaddimāt), the direction of evidence (wajh al-dalāla), and how to repel doubts. Once the student grasps what the teacher conveyed, he moves to the conclusion—this movement is itself a form of reflection. Similarly, inspiration (ilhām) cannot be trusted until it is verified that it is from God, and such verification can only be achieved through reflective inquiry (naẓar). Thus, in all cases, reflection is indispensable.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage dismisses teaching, inspiration, etc. as invalid ways to gain knowledge.
Correction: It does not dismiss them; it shows that they all inherently involve reflection (nazar).

Checkpoint

B) Because the student must mentally move from premises to conclusion after guidance
C) Because the teacher's statements are themselves reflective

Key Takeaway
Reflection (nazar) is indispensable for all forms of knowledge, even those that appear to bypass it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Obligation of Knowledge: General vs. Detailed Proof
Defines two levels of obligation for knowledge through reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between personal and communal obligations in seeking knowledge through reasoning, and the roles of general and detailed proofs.

Core Idea
Knowledge through reasoning is a personal duty for all; detailed proof is a communal duty for some.

Sanusi clarifies two levels of obligation for theological knowledge rooted in reasoning. He first reassures that taqlīd (blind acceptance) is permissible for some, but then establishes that every responsible Muslim must have a general proof to rise above taqlīd. Additionally, acquiring detailed proofs to defend and teach the faith is a communal responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the permissibility of taqlīd
The passage begins by acknowledging that taqlīd is permissible for some but does not negate the obligation of reasoning. Recognize this as a balanced stance.
This sets the context for the obligations that follow.
2
Identify the personal obligation (farḍ ‘ayn)
Focus on the statement that knowledge through a general proof is a personal obligation for every mukallaf. This is the core of individual responsibility.
3
Identify the communal obligation (farḍ kifāya)
Note that knowledge through a detailed proof is a communal obligation, necessary for defending the faith and guiding others. This distinguishes between personal and societal duties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثبت جواز الاكتفاء بالتقليد في حق البعض فهو لا ينافي وجوب المعرفة بالنظر والاستدلال في الجملة. هذا والحق أن المعرفة بدليل إجمالي به يرفع الناظر عن حضيض التقليد فرض عين لا مخرج عنه لأحد من المكلفين، وبدليل تفصيلي يتمكن معه من إزاحة الشبه وإلزام المنكرين، وإرشاد المسترشدين فرض كفاية، لا بد من أن يقوم به البعض.
The permissibility of being content with taqlīd for some people is established, so it does not contradict the obligation of knowledge through reason (naẓar) and inference (istidlāl) in general. This and the truth is that knowledge through a general proof (dalīl ijmālī) by which the viewer rises from the lowly level of taqlīd is a personal obligation (farḍ ‘ayn) from which no mukallaf has an exit; and through a detailed proof (dalīl tafsīlī) by which he is able to remove doubts, compel the deniers, and guide the seekers is a communal obligation (farḍ kifāya), which some must undertake.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage defines the obligation of knowledge (ma‘rifa) by distinguishing between two types of proofs. It begins by stating that reliance on taqlīd (blind acceptance) is permissible for some, but this does not negate the obligation of knowledge through reasoning (naẓar) and inference (istidlāl) in general. Then it asserts the truth: knowledge through a general proof (dalīl ijmālī) that lifts the seeker from the low level of taqlīd is a personal obligation (farḍ ‘ayn) for every morally responsible individual (mukallaf), with no excuse. Knowledge through a detailed proof (dalīl tafsīlī) that enables one to dispel doubts, refute deniers, and guide seekers is a communal obligation (farḍ kifāya) that some must fulfill.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqlīd (blind imitation) is absolutely forbidden and has no place in Islam.
Correction: The passage states that taqlīd is permissible for some people, but this does not negate the obligation of knowledge through reasoning for others. It is a nuanced position.

Checkpoint

A) It is a communal obligation (farḍ kifāya)
B) It is a personal obligation (farḍ ‘ayn)
C) It is recommended (mandūb)
D) It is forbidden (ḥarām)

Key Takeaway
Every Muslim must have a general proof to believe, but detailed proofs are required from the community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Third Objection: Knowledge Without Reasoning Refuted
Refutes the claim that knowledge can be obtained without reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why all claimed alternatives to reasoning—teaching, inspiration, infallible imam, and inner purification—ultimately depend on reasoning.

Core Idea
Every path to knowledge, hidden or apparent, runs through reasoning.

The objection attempts to bypass reasoning, but the answer reveals that each alternative secretly relies on it. This lesson unpacks that refutation, showing the inescapability of reasoning for acquiring non-self-evident knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the four alternatives
Read the objection which lists four ways knowledge might be obtained without reasoning: teaching, inspiration, infallible imam, and inner purification. Note which groups advocate each.
These are the materialists, Brahmins, Shia, and Sufis respectively.
2
See how each is dismantled
Follow the answer's argument for each alternative. The key is that all depend on reasoning, whether apparent or hidden. Notice the analogy of the eye and light.
Teaching assists but does not replace reasoning; inspiration requires verification; an imam's authority traces back to reason; inner purification presupposes prior certainty.
3
Grasp the final concession
Understand the concluding point: even if some rare individuals could know without reasoning, the argument holds for the majority. Reasoning remains the general path.
This safeguards the argument from extreme counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أنا لا نسلم أن المعرفة الكاملة لا تحصل إلا بالنظر، بل قد تحصل بالتعليم على ما يراه الملاحدة، أو بالإلهام على ما يراه البراهمة، أو بقول الإمام المعصوم على ما يراه الشيعة، أو بتصفية الباطن بالرياضات والمجاهدات على ما يراه المتصوفة. والجواب : أنا نعلم بالضرورة أن تحصيل غير الضروري من العلوم يفتقر إلى نظر ما ظاهر أو خفي، أما التعليم فظاهر لأنه ليس إلا إعانة للغفل بالإرشاد إلى المقدمات ودفع الشكوك والشبهات، وقد شبهوا نظر البصيرة بنظر الباصرة، وقول المعلم بالضوء الحسي، وكما لا يتم الإبصار إلا بهما لا تتم المعرفة إلا بالنظر والتعليم. وكذا الكلام في المعصوم، إذ لا يكفي في صدقه إخبار معصوم آخر ما لم ينته إلى نظر العقل. وأما الإلهام فلأنه لا يثق به صاحبه ما لم يعلم أنه من الله تعالى، وذلك بالنظر وإن لم يقدر على العبارة عنه. وأما تصفية الباطن فلأنه لا عبرة بها إلا بعد طمأنينة النفس في المعرفة، وذلك بالنظر. على أنه لو ثبت حصول المعرفة بدون النظر لم يضرنا، لأنا إنما ندعي الاحتياج إليه في حق الأغلب، وهذا لا يمنع لظهور كونه طريق العامة.
The third: We do not grant that complete knowledge is only obtained through reasoning; rather it may be obtained through teaching as held by the materialists (malāḥida), or through inspiration (ilhām) as held by the Brahmins (barāhima), or through the saying of the infallible imam (imām ma‘ṣūm) as held by the Shia, or through purification of the inner self (taṣfiyat al-bāṭin) by spiritual exercises and struggles (riyāḍāt wa mujāhadāt) as held by the Sufis. The answer: We know necessarily (bi-ḍarūra) that acquiring non-necessary sciences requires some reasoning (naẓar), whether apparent or hidden. As for teaching (ta‘līm), it is apparent because it is only assistance to the heedless by guiding to premises and repelling doubts and obscurities. They have likened the insight of the inner eye (naẓar al-baṣīra) to the sight of the visual eye (naẓar al-bāṣira), and the saying of the teacher to sensory light; just as vision is not completed except by both, knowledge is not completed except by reasoning and teaching. The same applies to the infallible imam, for the report of another infallible is not sufficient for his truthfulness unless it terminates in intellectual reasoning. As for inspiration, its possessor does not trust it unless he knows that it is from Allah, and that is through reasoning, even if he is unable to express it. As for inner purification, it is not considered except after the soul's tranquility in knowledge, and that is through reasoning. Moreover, if the occurrence of knowledge without reasoning were established, it would not harm us, for we only claim the need for it for the majority, and this does not prevent it from being the general path.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its refutation. The objector claims that complete knowledge can be obtained without reasoning, citing four alternative ways: teaching (ta‘līm) as held by materialists, inspiration (ilhām) as held by Brahmins, the infallible imam as held by Shia, and inner purification (taṣfiyat al-bāṭin) as held by Sufis. The answer begins with a necessary truth: all non-self-evident sciences require some reasoning, either apparent or hidden. Teaching is merely guidance to premises and removal of doubts; it is likened to sensory light and vision—just as eyesight needs both the eye and light, knowledge needs both reasoning and teaching. The same logic applies to the infallible imam: an imam's truthfulness is not established simply by another imam's report unless it is based on intellectual reasoning. Inspiration is only trusted after knowing it is from God, which requires reasoning, even if one cannot articulate it. Inner purification is irrelevant without prior certainty in knowledge, which again depends on reasoning. Finally, even if knowledge could occur without reasoning for some, it does not harm the claim because the argument is for the majority, and reasoning remains the general path.

Key Terms
معرفة
نظر
تعليم
إلهام
معصوم
تصفية الباطن
براهمة
ملاحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that reasoning is unnecessary because knowledge can be obtained through teaching, inspiration, an infallible imam, or inner purification.
Correction: The answer shows that each of these alternatives depends on reasoning—teaching assists reasoning, inspiration and imam's authority require rational verification, and inner purification presupposes prior rational certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is necessary for acquiring non-self-evident sciences?
A) Only teaching
B) Only inspiration
C) Only the infallible imam
D) Some form of reasoning

Key Takeaway
The necessity of reasoning for acquiring knowledge is universal; even apparent alternatives like teaching, inspiration, or an infallible imam ultimately depend on reasoning.

Fourth Objection: Absolute vs. Conditional Obligation of Knowledge
Objection and refutation on the absoluteness of knowledge obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to the unconditional obligation of knowledge and how the refutation distinguishes between absolute and conditional obligation.

Core Idea
Absolute obligation does not mean obligation in all states, but given the prerequisite and its absence.

The fourth objection challenges the claim that knowledge is absolutely obligatory, arguing it is only conditional on doubt or lack of knowledge. The refutation clarifies the true meaning of absolute obligation by drawing on fiqh examples like fasting and pilgrimage. This passage sharpens our understanding of how theological duties can be both absolute and conditional.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the objection
Read the objection carefully: it defines absolute obligation as obligation in every circumstance and then argues that knowledge is conditional on doubt or absence of knowledge, because there is no obligation when knowledge is already present.
Notice the objector's assumption that absolute obligation excludes all conditions.
2
Examine the refutation's core idea
The answer explains that absolute obligation does not mean obligation in all possible states, but rather obligation given the existence of its prerequisite and its non-existence. This is a key distinction.
3
Consider the fiqh examples
The examples of fasting (conditional on being resident) and Hajj (conditional on ability) show that obligations can be absolute in one aspect and conditional in another without contradiction.
Relate this back to knowledge: the condition of doubt or ignorance is a prerequisite, not a negation of absoluteness.

الرابع : أنا لا نسلم أن المعرفة واجب مطلق، فإن معناه الوجوب على كل تقدير، ووجوب المعرفة مقيد بحال الشك، أي تردد الذهن في النسبة، أو بحال عدم المعرفة، للقطع بأنه لا وجوب حال حصول المعرفة بالفعل، لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل. والجواب : أن ليس معنى الوجوب على كل تقدير عموم التقادير والأحوال، وإلا لما كان شيء من الواجبات واجباً مطلقاً، إذ لا يجب على تقدير الإتيان به. ولأن وجوب الصوم مثلاً مطلق بالقياس إلى النية، حتى يجب مقيد بالقياس إلى كون المكلف مقيماً غير مسافر، حتى لا تجب الإقامة. وكذا وجوب الحج مقيد بالاستطاعة، فلا يجب تحصيلها مطلقاً بالنسبة إلى الإحرام ونحوه من الشرائط، فيجب. بل معناه الوجوب على تقدير وجود المقدمة وعدمها. ووجوب المعرفة ليس مقيداً بالنظر بمعنى أنه لو نظر تجب المعرفة، وإلا فلا يكون مطلقاً. وأما بالنسبة إلى الشك أو عدم المعرفة فمقيد، إذ لا وجوب على العارف، فلا يكون تحصيل الشك وعدم المعرفة واجباً. ويندفع إشكال آخر هو نقض ...
The fourth: We do not grant that knowledge is absolutely obligatory (wājib muṭlaq), for its meaning is obligation in every circumstance (taqdīr), and the obligation of knowledge is conditioned by the state of doubt—i.e., the mind's hesitation concerning the relation—or by the state of absence of knowledge, because it is certain that there is no obligation when knowledge is already actual, since acquiring what is already possessed is impossible. The answer: The meaning of obligation in every circumstance is not generality of all circumstances and states; otherwise nothing of the obligations would be absolutely obligatory, since it is not obligatory in the circumstance of having performed it. And because the obligation of fasting, for example, is absolute with respect to intention, while it is conditional with respect to the mukallaf being resident not traveling, so that residency is not obligatory. Likewise, the obligation of pilgrimage is conditional on ability (istiṭā‘a), so obtaining ability is not absolutely obligatory with respect to iḥrām and similar conditions; rather it is obligatory. But its meaning is obligation in the circumstance of the existence of the prerequisite (muqaddima) and its non-existence. And the obligation of knowledge is not conditional on reasoning in the sense that if one reasons, knowledge becomes obligatory; otherwise it would not be absolute. As for the state of doubt or absence of knowledge, it is a condition, so there is no obligation on the knower, and thus obtaining doubt and absence of knowledge is not obligatory. And another problem is resolved, which is...
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and its refutation. The objector argues that knowledge is not absolutely obligatory because absolute obligation means obligation in every circumstance, whereas the obligation of knowledge is conditional upon the state of doubt or lack of knowledge; knowledge is not obligatory when already present, since acquiring what is already possessed is impossible. The answer clarifies that absolute obligation does not imply obligation in every circumstance without exception; otherwise no obligation would be absolute, because it is not obligatory when already performed. Examples: fasting is absolute with respect to intention, but conditional on being resident; pilgrimage is conditional on ability. Rather, absolute obligation means the obligation exists given the existence of its prerequisite and its non-existence (i.e., if the prerequisite is present, the obligation is binding; if absent, it is not). The obligation of knowledge is not conditional on reasoning in the sense that knowledge only becomes obligatory when one reasons; that would make it non-absolute. As for the condition of doubt or absence of knowledge, it is a condition for the obligation, not a negation of absoluteness. The answer ends by stating it resolves another problem (the text cuts off).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absolute obligation means obligation in all states without any condition.
Correction: Absolute obligation means obligation given the existence of its prerequisite and its non-existence; it can coexist with certain conditions.

Checkpoint
What does the answer say is the correct meaning of 'absolute obligation' (wājib muṭlaq)?
A) Obligation in every circumstance without exception
B) Obligation given the existence of its prerequisite and its non-existence
C) Obligation only when the act is performed
D) Obligation that cannot be conditional

Key Takeaway
Absolute obligation of knowledge means it is binding when the prerequisite (doubt or ignorance) is present, not that it applies in all possible states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you using your current state of ignorance or doubt as an excuse to postpone learning about your faith, even though this lesson shows that exactly that state makes knowledge obligatory upon you right now? What will you do starting today?
?
Do you assume that because you already know the basics of Islam, you are excused from deeper study, forgetting that knowledge is only obligatory in the absence of knowledge? Will you examine your heart for areas where you have settled for ignorance?
?
In what area of your life have you been waiting for a perfect state to seek knowledge, instead of recognizing that the obligation arises precisely in your current state of uncertainty? What step will you take today to fulfill that obligation?
